Divine Decree
intermediate
8 min
The Cause of Necessity: Eliminating Self and Servant, Establishing God's Attribute
Elimination of alternatives to establish divine power as cause

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the necessity of human actions cannot be due to the action itself or the servant, and must be due to an attribute of God, which is power or will.

Core Idea
The necessity of actions is eternal, so it cannot originate from the temporal servant.

Building on the premise that necessity requires a cause, the author now examines the possible sources of that necessity. He argues it cannot be intrinsic to the action (since it is possible) nor from the servant (since the necessity is eternal), so it must be from God. He then eliminates divine knowledge as a direct cause, concluding that power or will is the effective agent.

Guided Path
1
Understand the options: self, servant, or God
The passage argues that the necessity of the action must be caused either by the action itself, the servant, or God.
2
Eliminate self-necessity
The action is possible in itself, so it cannot be necessary by itself. This is a basic kalam principle.
Possible things are not inherently necessary.
3
Eliminate servant-necessity
The necessity is eternal (judged in pre-eternity), so it cannot be caused by the temporal servant.
The text says the action is 'judged in pre-eternity as necessary'.
4
Eliminate knowledge, leaving power/will
The author shows that knowledge cannot be the cause because it follows the known. The cause must be an attribute that tilts the possible, which is power or will.
This step is crucial: knowledge is not creative, power and will are.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
البرهان السابع
أفعال العباد معلومة الوقوع لله تعالى . أو معلومة اللا وقوع . وكل ما هو معلوم الوقوع ، كان واجب الوقوع . وكل ما هو معلوم اللا وقوع ، كان واجب اللا وقوع . وكل ذلك قد تقدم تقريره . فنقول : ومتى كان الأمر كذلك ، وجب أن يكون ذلك الوجوب إما بسبب : أن الله رجع وجودها على عدمها وإما بسبب أن الله تعالى خلق ما يوجب وقوعها ، إما بواسطة ، أو بغير واسطة . وعلى كل هذه التقديرات ، فمقصودنا حاصل . والذي يدل على صحة هذه المقدمة : أن فعل العبد لما كان واجب الوقوع في ذلك الوقت المعين. فإما أن يكون وجوبه لذاته . وهو محال . لأن الممكن لذاته ، لا يكون واجباً لذاته. وإما أن يكون وجوبه لغيره وذلك الغير . إما العبد ، وإما الله . والأول باطل . لأن الفعل الذي علم الله وقوعه في الوقت الفلاني محكوم عليه في الأزل بأنه واجب أن يقع في الوقت الفلاني فلا يزال . فالوجوب بهذا التفسير حاصل في الأزل ، بما لا يكون حاصلاً في الأزل : محال . ولما بطل هذا ، ثبت : أن المؤثر في حصول الوجوب بالتفسير الذي ذكرناه : صفة من صفات الله تعالى . وتلك الصفة . إما العلم ، وإما غيره . والأول باطل . لأن العلم يتعلق بالمعلوم على ما هو عليه ، وهو يتبع المعلوم ولا يستتبعه . فيمتنع أن يكون المقتضي لهذا الوجوب هو علم الله . فثبت : أن المقتضي لهذا الوجوب : صفة من صفات الله ، باشرها في ترجيح أحد طرفي الممكن على الآخر ، وتلك الصفة ليست إلا القدرة أو الإرادة . فثبت : أن المؤثر في وقوع أفعال العباد ، ليس إلا قدرة الله إما ابتداء أو بواسطة . وذلك هو المطلوب . وهذا أيضاً برهان شريف المطلوب . إلا أن البرهان المبني على اعتبار أحوال المتناقضين أشرف من هذا البرهان . وذلك لأنا في هذا البرهان نفتقر لإقامة الدلالة على كونه تعالى عالم بجميع المعلومات . حتى يثبت لنا عند ذلك : أنه تعالى كان عالماً بأفعال العباد قبل وقوعها . وهذه المقدمة إن أخذناها على سبيل أن الخصم شاهد عليها ، كان هذا الكلام الزاما برهانياً . فإن حاولنا إثباتها بالدليل ، تعذر ذلك علينا . لأن المعتمد في إثبات أنه تعالى عالم بأفعال العباد قبل وقوعها : هو أنه تعالى مقدر لها وخالق لها . فوجب أن يكون عالماً بها . فنحن إنما أثبتنا كونه تعالى عالماً بها قبل وقوعها ، لأنه ثبت كونه تعالى
The Seventh Proof. Human actions are known to God as occurring or as not occurring. And everything known to occur is necessary to occur. And everything known not to occur is necessary not to occur. And all that has been previously established. So we say: When the matter is thus, then that necessity must be either because God preferred their existence over their non-existence, or because God created what necessitates their occurrence, either directly or without an intermediary. And according to all these estimations, our goal is attained. And that which indicates the soundness of this premise is: Since the servant's action is necessary to occur at that specific time, then either its necessity is due to itself—and that is impossible, because the possible in itself is not necessary in itself—or its necessity is due to something else, and that something else is either the servant or God. The first is false, because the action which God knows will occur at such-and-such a time is judged in pre-eternity as being necessary to occur at that time, so it continues. Thus, the necessity according to this interpretation is realized in pre-eternity, but that which is not realized in pre-eternity is impossible. When this is invalidated, it is established that the effective agent in bringing about the necessity according to the interpretation we have mentioned is an attribute of God. That attribute is either knowledge or something else. The first is false, because knowledge relates to the known as it is, and it follows the known and does not cause it. So it is impossible that the cause of this necessity is God's knowledge. So it is established that the cause of this necessity is an attribute of God that directly tilts one of the two sides of the possible over the other, and that attribute is only power or will. So it is established that the effective agent in the occurrence of human actions is only God's power, either directly or through an intermediary. And that is what is sought. This also is a noble proof of what is sought. However, the proof based on considering the states of two contradictories is more noble than this proof. That is because in this proof we need to establish the evidence that God is knowledgeable of all things, so that we may thereby prove that He was knowledgeable of human actions before their occurrence. If we take this premise as a concession from the opponent, then this statement becomes a dialectical refutation. But if we attempt to prove it by evidence, that becomes difficult for us, because the basis for proving that God is knowledgeable of human actions before their occurrence is that He is their predeterminer and creator; so He must be knowledgeable of them. Thus, we only prove that He is knowledgeable of them before their occurrence because it is established that He is
Source pages p. 64

Commentary
This passage presents the seventh proof (البرهان السابع) for the predestination of human actions. The author begins by stating a premise: God knows all human actions as either occurring or not occurring. Whatever He knows to occur is necessary to occur, and whatever He knows not to occur is necessary not to occur. This necessity must have a cause. The author then argues that this cause is either God's preference (ارجح? رجع likely means رجح) or His creation of what necessitates the action. The proof then proceeds by examining the nature of the action's necessity: it cannot be necessary by itself (because it is possible), nor can it be necessary by the servant (because the necessity is eternal, predating the servant). Thus, the necessity must come from an attribute of God. The author then eliminates knowledge as the cause, because knowledge follows the known and does not cause it. Therefore, the cause must be either power (القدرة) or will (الإرادة). The conclusion is that God's power is the effective agent in causing human actions, directly or through intermediaries. The author then evaluates this proof compared to another (the proof based on contradictory states), noting that this proof requires first establishing God's knowledge of future actions, which itself depends on His being the creator and predeterminer. If the opponent concedes this knowledge, the proof works as a dialectical argument; otherwise, it is difficult to prove independently. This shows the author's methodological awareness.

Methodological Note: The Proof's Dependence on Divine Knowledge
Methodological critique of the proof's reliance on divine knowledge

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the author's critique of this proof: it requires establishing God's knowledge of future actions, which itself depends on His being the creator.

Core Idea
This proof is dialectical; it works if the opponent concedes God's knowledge.

After concluding the proof, the author offers a methodological reflection. He notes that this proof is less noble than another proof because it relies on the premise that God knows future actions. If the opponent agrees to that premise, the proof is a valid dialectical argument. But if one tries to prove that premise independently, it becomes circular because God's knowledge of future actions is deduced from His being the creator.

Guided Path
1
Understand the author's comparison
The author compares this proof to one based on contradictory states (أحوال المتناقضين), saying the latter is nobler.
2
Identify the prerequisite: God's knowledge of future actions
This proof requires first establishing that God knows all human actions before they occur.
The author says 'we need to establish that God is knowledgeable of all things'.
3
Grasp the dialectical nature
If the opponent concedes God's knowledge, the proof works as a dialectical argument. Otherwise, proving it independently is difficult because the standard proof of God's knowledge is that He is the creator.
The passage is cut off, but the point is that the proof's strength depends on a conceded premise.

البرهان السابع
أفعال العباد معلومة الوقوع لله تعالى . أو معلومة اللا وقوع . وكل ما هو معلوم الوقوع ، كان واجب الوقوع . وكل ما هو معلوم اللا وقوع ، كان واجب اللا وقوع . وكل ذلك قد تقدم تقريره . فنقول : ومتى كان الأمر كذلك ، وجب أن يكون ذلك الوجوب إما بسبب : أن الله رجع وجودها على عدمها وإما بسبب أن الله تعالى خلق ما يوجب وقوعها ، إما بواسطة ، أو بغير واسطة . وعلى كل هذه التقديرات ، فمقصودنا حاصل . والذي يدل على صحة هذه المقدمة : أن فعل العبد لما كان واجب الوقوع في ذلك الوقت المعين. فإما أن يكون وجوبه لذاته . وهو محال . لأن الممكن لذاته ، لا يكون واجباً لذاته. وإما أن يكون وجوبه لغيره وذلك الغير . إما العبد ، وإما الله . والأول باطل . لأن الفعل الذي علم الله وقوعه في الوقت الفلاني محكوم عليه في الأزل بأنه واجب أن يقع في الوقت الفلاني فلا يزال . فالوجوب بهذا التفسير حاصل في الأزل ، بما لا يكون حاصلاً في الأزل : محال . ولما بطل هذا ، ثبت : أن المؤثر في حصول الوجوب بالتفسير الذي ذكرناه : صفة من صفات الله تعالى . وتلك الصفة . إما العلم ، وإما غيره . والأول باطل . لأن العلم يتعلق بالمعلوم على ما هو عليه ، وهو يتبع المعلوم ولا يستتبعه . فيمتنع أن يكون المقتضي لهذا الوجوب هو علم الله . فثبت : أن المقتضي لهذا الوجوب : صفة من صفات الله ، باشرها في ترجيح أحد طرفي الممكن على الآخر ، وتلك الصفة ليست إلا القدرة أو الإرادة . فثبت : أن المؤثر في وقوع أفعال العباد ، ليس إلا قدرة الله إما ابتداء أو بواسطة . وذلك هو المطلوب . وهذا أيضاً برهان شريف المطلوب . إلا أن البرهان المبني على اعتبار أحوال المتناقضين أشرف من هذا البرهان . وذلك لأنا في هذا البرهان نفتقر لإقامة الدلالة على كونه تعالى عالم بجميع المعلومات . حتى يثبت لنا عند ذلك : أنه تعالى كان عالماً بأفعال العباد قبل وقوعها . وهذه المقدمة إن أخذناها على سبيل أن الخصم شاهد عليها ، كان هذا الكلام الزاما برهانياً . فإن حاولنا إثباتها بالدليل ، تعذر ذلك علينا . لأن المعتمد في إثبات أنه تعالى عالم بأفعال العباد قبل وقوعها : هو أنه تعالى مقدر لها وخالق لها . فوجب أن يكون عالماً بها . فنحن إنما أثبتنا كونه تعالى عالماً بها قبل وقوعها ، لأنه ثبت كونه تعالى

The argument that human action without a sufficient cause leads to compulsion
Two divisions showing indeterminacy leads to pure compulsion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the two divisions of this argument demonstrate that any alternative to the Ash'ari position on human action leads to compulsion (jabr).

Core Idea
Without a sufficient cause, human action collapses into pure compulsion.

This passage presents a rigorous two-part argument that challenges any view denying that a sufficient cause necessitates action. The author shows that if the act's occurrence is not determined by a condition added by the servant, it becomes mere coincidence, which is a form of compulsion. The alternative—that an additional condition is needed—leads either to an infinite regress or to the necessity of the act, both of which imply compulsion. Understanding this argument is key to grasping the Ash'ari position on causality and human agency.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once slowly, then the translation. Note the two main divisions (qism) introduced by the author.
The passage is dense; it may help to break it into sections.
2
Identify the first division
The first scenario: the act sometimes occurs, sometimes not, without any distinguishing factor from the servant. What is the conclusion? The act becomes pure coincidence, which the author calls 'pure compulsion'.
Coincidence here means no real agency—thus jabr.
3
Trace the second division
The second scenario: the servant and conditions are insufficient, so something else must be added. Follow the two sub-branches: if the added thing is the servant's act (impossible infinite regress) or God's act (then act is either necessary or the division repeats).
The argument uses a reductio ad absurdum.
4
Understand the conclusion
The author concludes that when all necessary conditions for influence (mu'thiriyya) are present, the act necessarily occurs; when any condition is missing, the act is impossible. This entails jabr.
5
Reflect on the significance
Ask yourself: Why does this argument matter for the debate on free will and predestination? How does it support the Ash'ari view?
The argument is internal to kalam, showing that the alternative position collapses into compulsion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بل يجوز أن يحصل الفعل تارة ، وأن لا يحصل أخرى ، ولم يكن تميز حصول الفعل عن لا حصوله بأمر آخر يضمه العبد إلى المجموع الذي كان حاصلا . فحينئذ يكون حصول الفعل محض الاتفاق . وهذا أيضاً محض الجبر وأما القسم الثاني : وهو أن يقال : ذات العبد مع مجموع تلك الأمور غير كاف في كونها مصدراً لذلك الفعل . فحينئذ لا بد من ضم شيء آخر إليه ، وذلك الأمر الآخر إن كان فعلا له فهو محال وإلا لزم أن يحصل فعل قبل حصول أول الأفعال وذلك محال وإن لم يكن فعلاً له بل كان فعلا الله تعالى فعند حصوله قد تم كل ما لا بد منه في كونه مصدراً لأفعاله فإما أن يكون صدور أفعاله عنه واجباً أو لا يكون وحينئذ يعود التقسيم المذكور بعينه فثبت أن حصول الفعل عند حصول كل ما لا بد منه في المؤثرية واجب وثبت أن عند فقدان كل تلك الأمور وعند فقدان بعضها يكون صدور الفعل عن العبد ممتنعاً وعلى هذا التقدير فإنه يكون القول بالجبر لازماً وبالله التوفيق
Rather, it is possible that the act occurs sometimes and does not occur other times, and the distinction between the occurrence of the act and its non-occurrence was not due to another matter that the servant adds to the totality that was already present. Then the occurrence of the act is pure coincidence. And this also is pure compulsion (jabr). As for the second division: that it is said: the essence of the servant together with the totality of those matters is insufficient for it to be the source of that act. Then there must be an addition of something else to it, and that other thing, if it is an act of his, it is impossible, otherwise it would necessitate that an act occur before the occurrence of the first of acts, and that is impossible. And if it is not an act of his, but rather an act of God Almighty, then upon its occurrence everything necessary for him to be a source of his acts has been completed. Then either the emanation of his acts from him is necessary or it is not, and then the aforementioned division returns exactly. So it is established that the occurrence of the act upon the attainment of everything necessary for influence (al-mu'thiriyya) is necessary, and it is established that when all those matters are absent, and when some of them are absent, the emanation of the act from the servant is impossible. And on this estimation, then the statement of compulsion (al-jabr) becomes necessary. And with God is success.
Source pages p. 67

Commentary
This passage presents a two-part argument to demonstrate that any alternative to the Ash'ari position on human action leads to compulsion (jabr). The first division: if the act's occurrence is not distinguished from its non-occurrence by something the servant adds to the already present conditions, then the act's occurrence is pure chance, which is equivalent to compulsion because the agent has no real control. The second division: if the servant's essence and the conditions are insufficient, then something else must be added. If that something is the servant's own act, an infinite regress follows (an act before the first act). If it is God's act, then once it is present, all conditions for the servant to be a source of acts are complete; then either the act's emanation is necessary or not. If necessary, it is compulsion; if not, the same division recurs. Thus the conclusion is that when all necessary conditions for influence are present, the act necessarily occurs, and when any condition is lacking, the act is impossible. This entails compulsion (jabr).

Key Terms
الجبر (jabr)
الاتفاق (coincidence)
المؤثرية (influence)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Human actions can occur without any sufficient cause.
Correction: The argument shows that if an action occurs without a sufficient cause, it is pure coincidence, which is a form of compulsion, not genuine freedom.

Checkpoint
What does the passage conclude about the occurrence of an act when all necessary conditions for influence are present?
A) It is possible but not necessary.
B) It is necessary.
C) It is impossible.
D) It depends on the servant's choice.

Key Takeaway
When all necessary conditions for influence are present, the act necessarily occurs; otherwise it is impossible, making compulsion inevitable.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?
If every act is ultimately compelled by Allah's decree, how does that change the way you should think about your own responsibility? Will you use this knowledge to increase your dependence on Him or to justify your shortcomings?

Refutation of Objections: God's Power Over Servant's Actions
Refutation of ten objections and proof of God's power over the servant's action.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Al-Matalib refutes the ten objections raised by the Mu'tazila and establishes that God's power necessarily extends to the servant's actions.

Core Idea
The ten objections only bind the Mu'tazila; God's power covers all objects of power.

In this passage, the author concludes a critical argument about divine power. He addresses the ten objections previously raised against the Mu'tazila, showing they do not affect his own position. Then he constructs a proof that God's power must attach to the servant's action, based on the principle of likeness and the impossibility of preponderance without a determiner.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the literal translation. Note the structure: first a conclusion from a prior argument, then dismissal of objections, then a new proof.
The passage is logically dense.
2
Identify the two main parts
The passage has two parts: the response to the ten objections and the constructive proof of God's power. Find where the shift occurs.
The shift is after 'لم يلزم علينا تلك النقوض'.
3
Understand the objection dismissal
The author says the ten objections only apply to Mu'tazilite positions. Since Ash'aris do not hold those, the objections are irrelevant. Why is this a valid defense?
This is a classic Ash'ari move.
4
Follow the proof logic
The proof moves from: (1) the servant's action is like God's object of power, (2) what is valid for one is valid for its like, (3) so it is valid for God's power, (4) if valid, God's power must attach, otherwise preponderance without determiner, (5) so God is powerful over it.
This is a modal argument.
5
Reflect on the conclusion
The conclusion is that God is powerful over all objects of power, including the servant's action. How does this relate to the broader discussion of qadar?
It affirms divine omnipotence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لافتقر إلى معين آخر. ولزم التسلسل ، وهو محال . والقيد العدمي لا دخل له في التأثير. ولما سقط التعين ، لم يبق إلا أصل الماهية : وحينئذ يحصل التقريب . وأما النقوض العشرة التي أوردناها في هذه المقدمة على المعتزلة . فهي لازمة على مذاهبهم . وأما نحن إذ كنا لا نقول بتلك المذاهب ، لم يلزم علينا تلك النقوض . فثبت : أن مثل مقدور العبد مقدور الله تعالى ، وثبت : أن ما صح على الشيء ، صح على مثله ، فوجب القطع بأن مقدور العبد ، يصح أن يكون [مقدور] الله تعالى وإذا حصلت هذه الصلاحية ، وجب تعلق قدرة الله تعالى به. إذ لو لم تتعلق قدرة الله تعالى به ، مع أنه يصح [تعلق] قدرته به ، لكان تعلق قدرته ببعض المقدورات دون البعض : ترجيحاً لأحد طرفي الجانب على الآخر . وذلك لا يحصل إلا لمخصص قادر . فيلزم : أن لا الله تعالى قادراً على مقدوراته، إلا لأجل أن شيئاً آخر قدر عليه . ولما كان ذلك في حق الله تعالى محالاً ، علمنا : أنه قادر على جميع المقدورات ، فوجب القطع بكونه تعالى قادرا على مقدور العبد.
It would not need another determiner. And infinite regress would follow, which is impossible. The privative restriction has no effect in causation. When determination fell away, nothing remained except the essence of the quiddity, and then approximation occurs. As for the ten objections we raised in this introduction against the Mu'tazila, they are necessary according to their doctrines. But as for us, since we do not hold those doctrines, those objections do not follow against us. So it is established: that the like of the servant's object of power is an object of power of God Most High, and it is established: that whatever is valid for a thing is valid for its like. Thus it is necessary to decide that the servant's object of power is valid to be an object of power of God Most High. And when this validity obtains, it is necessary for the power of God Most High to be attached to it. For if the power of God Most High were not attached to it, while it is valid for His power to be attached to it, then His power being attached to some objects of power to the exclusion of others would be a preponderance of one side over the other. And that does not occur except through a powerful determiner. So it would follow that God Most High is not powerful over His objects of power except because something else determined it. And since that is impossible with respect to God Most High, we know that He is powerful over all objects of power, so it is necessary to decide that He Most High is powerful over the servant's object of power.
Source pages p. 79

Commentary
This passage is a rebuttal of objections and a constructive proof. It begins by concluding a prior argument: if determination is removed, only the essence (mahiya) remains, leading to approximation. Then it addresses ten objections raised earlier against the Mu'tazila. The author states that those objections only apply to the Mu'tazila's doctrines, not to his own school (Ash'ari). Because his school does not hold those problematic doctrines, the objections do not apply. From this, he establishes two principles: the servant's object of power is like an object of power of God, and what is valid for one thing is valid for its like. Therefore, the servant's action is valid to be an object of God's power. Once this validity is established, God's power must be attached to it. Otherwise, if God's power were not attached despite validity, that would imply a preponderance without a determiner, which is impossible. Hence God must be powerful over all objects of power, including the servant's action.

Key Terms
التسلسل
التعيين
الماهية

المقدور

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The ten objections disprove the Ash'ari view that God is powerful over human actions.
Correction: The author shows that the objections are founded on Mu'tazilite doctrines; since Ash'aris do not hold those, the objections do not apply.

Checkpoint
Why do the ten objections not apply to the author's own school?
A) Because the objections are invalid in themselves.
B) Because the author does not hold the Mu'tazilite doctrines that make them necessary.
C) Because the objections only apply to the Mu'tazila's definition of power.
D) Because the objections are about divine attributes, not human actions.

Key Takeaway
The ten objections only bind the Mu'tazila; the proof from likeness and preponderance establishes that God's power must extend to all actions, including the servant's.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you commit a sin, do you truly believe that God had power over your action? If so, how does this affect your understanding of your own responsibility and repentance? Today, ask yourself: Am I using God's omnipotence as an excuse to blame Him, or as a reason to seek His forgiveness and change?
?
The argument shows that denying God's power over your actions would imply a deficiency in God. Is there any area of your life where you act as if God is not in control—where you worry, scheme, or despair as if the outcome depends entirely on you? What would it mean for you to fully submit to His power in that area starting today?
?
The principle 'whatever is valid for a thing is valid for its like' is crucial to the proof. Are you consistent in applying sound reasoning to your beliefs about God, or do you tolerate contradictions that comfort you? Take one theological tension you avoid and resolve it now with the help of this lesson.

Divine Decree

Proof of Man's Ignorance of Action Details
Three-part proof of human ignorance of action details.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three-part proof that a human being is ignorant of the detailed components of his own voluntary actions.

Core Idea
You never know the full details of your own actions.

This passage from Al-Matalib al-Aliyah presents a rational proof that a person is unaware of the minutiae of his own voluntary movements. The proof examines three dimensions: the physical parts of the body, the spaces traversed, and the instants of time. By reflecting on our everyday experience, we can see that our ignorance is complete in all three respects.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation. Let the author's argument flow naturally.
The passage uses a repetitive structure: 'Also... also...' for each part.
2
Identify the three dimensions of ignorance
The proof presents three areas where a mover lacks knowledge: the number of body parts, the number of spaces traversed, and the number of time instants. Find each in the text.
3
Notice the term 'by necessity' (bi-al-darura)
The author claims we know by necessity that the mover is unaware. Reflect on why this is considered self-evident.
This is a key epistemological claim in kalam.
4
Understand how the conclusion is drawn
After presenting all three parts, the author concludes that the servant is absolutely unaware of the details of his actions. See how each part contributes.
5
Consider the theological implication
This proof is used in discussions of qadar (divine decree). Reflect on how human ignorance of action details affects the debate on free will and divine control.
The proof does not deny that the action occurs, only that the agent knows its details.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
من أجزاء كثيرة موجودة بالفعل . أما عند من يثبت الجوهر الفرد ، فلا شك فيه . وأما عند من ينكره ، فلا شك أنه معترف بأن مجموع البدن ، مؤلف من الأعضاء البسيطة - أعني العظام والغضاريف والأعصاب والعضلات والرباطات ، إلى غير ذلك من الأعضاء البسيطة - فإذا حرك الانسان بدنه ، فلا معني لهذا التحريك إلا أنه حرك مجموع تلك الأجزاء ، لكنا نعلم بالضرورة أنه البتة غير عالم بأعداد تلك الأعضاء. وأيضاً : فلا شك أنه لما حرك بدنه ، فقد نقل تلك الجثة من حيز إلى حيز ، ومر بما بين الطرفين ، مع أنه غير عالم بأعداد تلك الأحياز فلا يمكنه أن يعلم ما بين مبدأ تلك الحركة إلى منتهاها . وأيضاً : فلا شك أن تلك الحركة وقعت في مقدار معين من الزمان، وذلك القدر المعين من الزمان ، مركب من آنات متتالية متعاقبة. وهو البتة لا يعلم مقدار الزمان ، ولا عدد الآنات ، التي منها تركب ذلك الزمان . فثبت بما ذكرنا : أن من انتقل من مكان إلى مكان ، فهو لم يعلم أن الأجزاء التي حركها . كم هي ؟ والأحياز التي منها تألفت تلك المسافة التي فيها وقعت الحركة . كم هي ؟ والآنات التي منها تألف الزمان ، الذي هو طرف لتلك الحركة. كم هي فثبت : أن العبد غير عالم بتفاصيل أفعاله البتة.
From many parts already existing. As for he who affirms the indivisible atom (al-jawhar al-fard), there is no doubt about it. And as for he who denies it, there is no doubt that he acknowledges that the sum total of the body is composed of simple organs – I mean bones, cartilages, nerves, muscles, and ligaments, up to other simple organs. So when a man moves his body, there is no meaning to this movement except that he moves the totality of those parts, but we know by necessity (bi-al-darura) that he is absolutely unaware of the numbers of those organs. Also: there is no doubt that when he moved his body, he transferred that mass from one space (hayyiz) to another, and passed through what is between the two extremes, while he is unaware of the numbers of those spaces, so he cannot know what is between the beginning of that movement and its end. Also: there is no doubt that that movement occurred in a specified amount of time, and that specified amount of time is composed of successive consecutive instants (anāt). And he is absolutely unaware of the amount of time, nor the number of instants from which that time is composed. So it is established by what we have mentioned: that whoever moves from one place to another, he does not know how many the parts he moved are, nor how many the spaces that composed that distance in which the movement occurred are, nor how many the instants that composed the time, which is the extent of that movement, are. So it is established: that the servant (al-‘abd) is absolutely unaware of the details of his actions.
Source pages p. 87

Commentary
This passage is a proof that a human being is ignorant of the detailed components of his own voluntary actions. The author presents a three-part argument, each part building on the previous. First, he considers the composition of the body. Whether one accepts the atomic theory (al-jawhar al-fard) or not, everyone agrees the body is composed of many simple parts (bones, cartilages, etc.). When a person moves his body, he moves the whole collection, but he does not know the exact number of those parts. Second, the author considers the spatial dimension. When the body moves, it traverses a distance composed of many individual spaces (ahiyaz). The mover is unaware of the number of these spaces, yet the movement occurs through them. Third, the author considers the temporal dimension. The movement takes a specific amount of time, which is composed of consecutive instants (anāt). The mover does not know the number of instants. From these three considerations, the author concludes that a person who moves from one place to another does not know the number of parts he moved, the number of spaces traversed, or the number of instants elapsed. Hence, the servant (al-‘abd) is absolutely unaware of the details of his actions. This proof is used in kalam to argue that human actions are not fully known to the agent, thus challenging the notion of complete human control over actions.

Key Terms
جوهر فرد
حيّز
آنات
الضرورة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think this proof denies human agency entirely, but it only denies detailed knowledge, not the act itself.
Correction: The proof does not claim that actions do not occur or that humans have no role; it only shows that humans lack full knowledge of the components of their actions.

Checkpoint
According to the proof, how many things does the mover not know the number of?
A) One: the parts of the body
B) Two: the parts and the spaces
C) Three: the parts, the spaces, and the instants
D) Four: the parts, spaces, instants, and the amount of force

Key Takeaway
The proof establishes that a human being is necessarily ignorant of the detailed components of his actions in three domains: parts, spaces, and time instants.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You go through each day performing countless actions—walking, speaking, typing—without ever pausing to consider the countless parts, spaces, and instants involved. This proof shows you are completely ignorant of those details. How does this undeniable ignorance make you feel about your claim that you are the independent doer of your deeds? Will you today whisper a prayer of reliance on Allah before your next action?
?
If you cannot fully know even the simplest physical movement you make, how can you ever be sure of the purity of your intentions or the perfection of your worship? Does this thought not drive you to seek forgiveness for every act, knowing that its reality is beyond your grasp? Commit right now to ask Allah for acceptance of your deeds, despite your ignorance.
?
Consider a time you judged someone else's action harshly. Did you know the complete details of what they did—the parts, spaces, and time instants that made up their movement? This proof reveals your ignorance. How does this realization soften your heart toward others? Will you resolve to leave all judgment to Allah, who alone knows the full reality of every action?

The Theologians on Slow Motion
Author presents the theologians' view on slow motion and critiques it.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the theologians' explanation that slow motion is a mixture of movements and rests, and the criticism that such an action requires detailed conscious intention which is absent in ordinary movement.

Core Idea
The theologians mix movement and rest to explain slowness, but that requires detailed intention we lack.

The argument from slow motion challenges human agency. Theologians explain slowness as a mixture of movement and rest. But if voluntary, the agent must consciously choose each space, which we know we don't. Let's examine this.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to grasp the theologians' position and the objection raised against it.
Notice the logical flow: observation, theory, conditional objection.
2
Understand the mixture theory
The theologians say slow motion arises from alternating movement and rest. Imagine walking: you move in some spaces and pause in others, creating an overall slower motion.
This view assumes that slowness is not a quality of motion itself but a result of composition.
3
Grasp the objection
The author argues that if such motion is voluntary, each specific space chosen for movement or rest must be intended with knowledge. Yet ordinary motion does not involve such detailed intention.
The objection relies on our self-evident lack of awareness of these specifications.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالث : إن الإنسان إذا تحرك . فلا شك أن حركته أبطأ من حركة الفلك . وللناس في هذا البطء مذهبان : أحدهما : مذهب المتكلمين : وهو أن الحركة البطيئة نشأت عن كونه متحركاً في بعض الأحياز، وساكناً في بعضها ، فامتزجت تلك الحركات بتلك السكنات ، فشاهد تلك الحركات المخلوطة بتلك السكنات : حركة بطيئة . إذا ثبت هذا ، فنقول : إذا كانت هذه الحركة فعلاً اختيارياً ، وجب القطع بأن الإنسان باختياره يتحرك في بعض الأحياز ، وباختياره يسكن في بعضها . لكن تخصيص بعض الأحياز بالحركة، والبعض بالسكون ، تخصيصاً بالقصد ، لا يمكن إلا بعد الشعور والعلم . لكنا نعلم بالضرورة : أن الإنسان إذا تحرك فإنه لم يخطر بباله أنه يتحرك في بعض الأحياز ، ويسكن في بعضها وكيف يقال : إنه باختياره فعل في بعض الأحياز حركة ، وفي بعضها سكوناً ؟ والمذهب الثاني : مذهب الفلاسفة : وهو أن الحركة البطيئة : حركة من أول المسافة إلى آخرها ، ولم يختلط بها شيء من السكونات . والبطء : كيفية قائمة بالحركة . وعلى هذا المذهب ، فالإشكال لازم من وجه آخر . وذلك أن مراتب البطء والسرعة في الحركات كثيرة متفاوتة. فإنه لا بطء ، إلا ويوجد ما هو أبطأ منه ، أو ما هو أسرع منه . فوقوع هذه المرتبة المعينة من البطء والسرعة ، دون سائر المراتب ، لا بد وأن يكون بالقصد . لأن القصد إلى إيقاع هذه المرتبة دون سائر المراتب : مشروط بالعلم بامتياز هذه المرتبة عن سائر المراتب . ومعلوم : أن النملة إذا تحركت ، فإنه لم يخطر ببالها امتياز تلك المرتبة من السرعة والبطء ، عن المرتبة التي تزداد عليها وتنتقص عنها ، فقدر تلك المرتبة من السرعة والبطء ، عن المرتبة التي تزداد عليها وتنتقص منها قدر قليل لا يحس الإنسان به. فإن الذي لا يقدر الإنسان على الإحساس به ، كيف تقدر النملة والدودة على الإحساس به ؟ وأيضاً : فعلى هذا المذهب لا بد وأن يكون البطء عرضاً قائماً بتلك الحركة . ففاعل تلك الحركة البطيئة فاعل لنوعين من الفعل
The third: If a man moves, there is no doubt that his movement is slower than the movement of the celestial sphere (falak). People have two schools (madhhabān) regarding this slowness (al-buṭ’): One of them: the school of the theologians (al-mutakallimīn): that the slow movement arose from him being moving in some spaces and stationary in others, so those movements mixed with those rests, and one observes those movements mixed with those rests as a slow movement. If this is established, we say: if this movement is a voluntary act (fi‘lan ikhtiyāriyyan), then it must be affirmed that the man by his choice (bi-ikhtiyārihi) moves in some spaces and by his choice rests in some. But specifying some spaces for movement and some for rest, a specification by intention (qasd), is only possible after sensation (shu‘ūr) and knowledge (‘ilm). But we know by necessity: that man, when he moves, it did not occur to his mind that he moves in some spaces and rests in some. How can it be said that by his choice he performed movement in some spaces and rest in others? The second school: the school of the philosophers (al-falāsifa): that slow movement is movement from the beginning of the distance to its end, and no rest mixed with it. And slowness: an accident (kayfiyya) subsisting (qā’im) in the movement. According to this school, the problem (ishkāl) follows from another aspect. That is, the levels (marātib) of slowness and speed in movements are many and varied. For there is no slowness except that there is what is slower than it, or what is faster than it. So the occurrence of this specific level of slowness and speed, to the exclusion of other levels, must be by intention (qasd). Because the intention to bring about this level to the exclusion of other levels is conditioned by knowledge of the distinction (imtiyāz) of this level from other levels. And it is known: that when an ant (namla) moves, it does not occur to its mind the distinction of that level of speed and slowness from the level that exceeds it or falls short of it. For that level of speed and slowness from the level that exceeds it or falls short of it is a small amount that a human does not perceive (lā yahiss). If what a human cannot perceive, how can an ant or a worm (dūda) perceive it? Also: according to this school, slowness must be an accident subsisting in that movement. So the agent (fā‘il) of that slow movement is an agent of two types of act...
Source pages p. 87

Commentary
This passage is the third argument in a series, presenting a difficulty for human agency based on the phenomenon of slow motion. The author first observes that human motion is slower than celestial motion. He then presents two schools that explain slowness. The first school is that of the theologians (mutakallimūn): they say slow motion is a mixture of movements and rests. The author then raises an objection: if such a motion is voluntary, then the agent must consciously choose in which spaces to move and in which to rest. But we know by necessity that when a person moves, he does not think about such detailed specifications of spaces. Hence, it cannot be truly voluntary. The second school is that of the philosophers: they say slow motion is a pure movement with an accident of slowness. The author then raises a different problem: the specific level of slowness (or speed) must be intended, but the agent (like an ant) is unaware of the minute distinctions between similar levels. Therefore, the agent cannot intend that specific level. Finally, the author adds that on the philosophers' view, slowness is an accident, and the agent would be producing two types of act (the motion and the accident), which is problematic. The passage is incomplete, but the argument points to the conclusion that human (or animal) actions are not fully under their control.

Key Terms
بطء
متكلمون
سكون
قصد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think the theologians' view is obviously correct and solves the problem of slowness.
Correction: The objection shows that their view leads to an unrealistic requirement of conscious intention, which ordinary motion lacks.

Checkpoint
According to the theologians, what causes slow motion?
A) An accident of slowness subsisting in the movement
B) Mixture of movements and rests
C) The influence of the celestial spheres
D) The agent's intention to move slowly

Key Takeaway
The theologians' mixture theory fails because it demands conscious intention for each component of motion, which is absent in ordinary human movement.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you perform a simple action like walking, do you ever consciously intend every moment of movement and every pause? If not, what does this say about the extent of your control over your own actions? How does this humbling reality affect your need to rely on Allah's decree?
?
The passage argues that detailed intention is absent in ordinary movement. Consider your daily choices – how many are the result of deliberate, moment-by-moment intention, and how many are automatic? What does this reveal about your dependence on Allah for even the simplest acts?
?
If your movements are not fully consciously intended, then how can you claim complete ownership of your actions? This lesson forces you to reconsider accountability. Are you willing to admit that your agency is limited, and that your ultimate reliance is on Allah's encompassing decree? How will this reshape your repentance and gratitude today?

The Philosophers on Slow Motion
Author presents the philosophers' view on slow motion and critiques it.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' view that slowness is an accident of movement, and the criticism that the specific degree of slowness requires intention and knowledge of minute differences beyond the agent's awareness.

Core Idea
Philosophers say slowness is an accident, but its specific level must be intended, yet even humans can't perceive such fine gradations.

The philosophers propose that slow motion is a pure continuous movement with an accident of slowness. But now the problem shifts: the specific degree of slowness must be chosen, yet the agent is unaware of such precise distinctions. Let's explore their view and its critique.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the philosophers' view and the subsequent objections. Notice the shift from the theologians' account to this new perspective.
The philosophers deny any mixture with rest; slowness is a quality of the motion itself.
2
Understand the accident view
The philosophers say that slow motion is a continuous movement from start to finish, and slowness is an accident (kayfiyya) subsisting in that movement.
This treats slowness as a real property, not a composite.
3
Grasp the objection about levels
The objection focuses on why this specific level of slowness occurred rather than another. Since levels are infinitely gradable, the agent must intend this precise level, which requires knowledge of its distinction from others. Yet even humans cannot perceive very fine differences, let alone an ant.
The ant example illustrates that agents lack the required awareness.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالث : إن الإنسان إذا تحرك . فلا شك أن حركته أبطأ من حركة الفلك . وللناس في هذا البطء مذهبان : أحدهما : مذهب المتكلمين : وهو أن الحركة البطيئة نشأت عن كونه متحركاً في بعض الأحياز، وساكناً في بعضها ، فامتزجت تلك الحركات بتلك السكنات ، فشاهد تلك الحركات المخلوطة بتلك السكنات : حركة بطيئة . إذا ثبت هذا ، فنقول : إذا كانت هذه الحركة فعلاً اختيارياً ، وجب القطع بأن الإنسان باختياره يتحرك في بعض الأحياز ، وباختياره يسكن في بعضها . لكن تخصيص بعض الأحياز بالحركة، والبعض بالسكون ، تخصيصاً بالقصد ، لا يمكن إلا بعد الشعور والعلم . لكنا نعلم بالضرورة : أن الإنسان إذا تحرك فإنه لم يخطر بباله أنه يتحرك في بعض الأحياز ، ويسكن في بعضها وكيف يقال : إنه باختياره فعل في بعض الأحياز حركة ، وفي بعضها سكوناً ؟ والمذهب الثاني : مذهب الفلاسفة : وهو أن الحركة البطيئة : حركة من أول المسافة إلى آخرها ، ولم يختلط بها شيء من السكونات . والبطء : كيفية قائمة بالحركة . وعلى هذا المذهب ، فالإشكال لازم من وجه آخر . وذلك أن مراتب البطء والسرعة في الحركات كثيرة متفاوتة. فإنه لا بطء ، إلا ويوجد ما هو أبطأ منه ، أو ما هو أسرع منه . فوقوع هذه المرتبة المعينة من البطء والسرعة ، دون سائر المراتب ، لا بد وأن يكون بالقصد . لأن القصد إلى إيقاع هذه المرتبة دون سائر المراتب : مشروط بالعلم بامتياز هذه المرتبة عن سائر المراتب . ومعلوم : أن النملة إذا تحركت ، فإنه لم يخطر ببالها امتياز تلك المرتبة من السرعة والبطء ، عن المرتبة التي تزداد عليها وتنتقص عنها ، فقدر تلك المرتبة من السرعة والبطء ، عن المرتبة التي تزداد عليها وتنتقص منها قدر قليل لا يحس الإنسان به. فإن الذي لا يقدر الإنسان على الإحساس به ، كيف تقدر النملة والدودة على الإحساس به ؟ وأيضاً : فعلى هذا المذهب لا بد وأن يكون البطء عرضاً قائماً بتلك الحركة . ففاعل تلك الحركة البطيئة فاعل لنوعين من الفعل
The third: If a man moves, there is no doubt that his movement is slower than the movement of the celestial sphere (falak). The first school is that of the theologians (mutakallimūn): they say slow motion is a mixture of movements and rests. The author then raises an objection: if such a motion is voluntary, then the agent must consciously choose in which spaces to move and in which to rest. But we know by necessity that when a person moves, he does not think about such detailed specifications of spaces. Hence, it cannot be truly voluntary. The second school is that of the philosophers: they say slow motion is a pure movement with an accident of slowness. The author then raises a different problem: the specific level of slowness (or speed) must be intended, but the agent (like an ant) is unaware of the minute distinctions between similar levels. Therefore, the agent cannot intend that specific level. Finally, the author adds that on the philosophers' view, slowness is an accident, and the agent would be producing two types of act (the motion and the accident), which is problematic. The passage is incomplete, but the argument points to the conclusion that human (or animal) actions are not fully under their control.

Key Terms
سرعة
فلاسفة
كيفية
عرض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the philosophers' view avoids the problem of the theologians by denying any mixture with rest.
Correction: The objection shows that the philosophers' view faces an even more subtle problem: the specific degree of slowness itself requires intention and knowledge beyond the agent's capacity.

Checkpoint
According to the philosophers, what is the nature of slowness in movement?
A) It is a mixture of movements and rests
B) It is an accident (kayfiyya) subsisting in the movement
C) It is a result of the celestial spheres' influence
D) It is an illusion of the observer

Key Takeaway
The philosophers' view fails because the specific level of slowness requires an intention that presupposes knowledge of fine distinctions beyond the agent's awareness.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you perform an action, do you ever consider the exact details that make it happen? How aware are you of God's decree in even the smallest movement of your body? This lesson shows that even an ant's movement requires knowledge beyond its capacity—so how can you claim to be fully in control of your own actions without relying on the Creator? Commit to recognizing His role in every detail of your daily movements.
?
The lesson forces you to admit that you are not aware of the precise speed of your own hand. If you cannot perceive that, how can you claim full authorship of your deeds? Reflect on a time you did something 'automatically'. Does that action still belong to you? Make a sincere resolve today to thank Allah for enabling even your smallest actions, and seek His forgiveness for attributing them to yourself.
?
In what areas of your life do you assume full intention and control? Could you be attributing to yourself something that only God can bring about? What would change if you recognized His role in the details of your existence? Challenge yourself: pick one routine action tomorrow, and before doing it, pause and acknowledge that only Allah has the knowledge and power to bring about that specific movement. Write down how this changes your experience.

The Ignorance of the Mu'tazilite 'Meaning'
Sanusi presents the fourth argument refuting Mu'tazilite human agency via ignorance of the 'meaning'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Mu'tazilite theory of a 'meaning' as the object of human power is untenable due to universal ignorance of such a meaning.

Core Idea
If even the agent is ignorant of the supposed 'meaning', how can it be the object of intentional action?

Sanusi now presents a powerful argument from ignorance: the Mu'tazilites claim that human power acts on a hidden 'meaning' rather than directly on the body's movement. But this 'meaning' is unknown to everyone, including the very agents who are supposed to intend it. Let's see how this undermines their entire theory.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing; then read the translation. Pay attention to the logical flow from the Mu'tazilite claim to the conclusion.
Notice the rhetorical escalation from beasts to scholars.
2
Identify the Mu'tazilite claim
Find where Sanusi states the Mu'tazilite doctrine: that the servant's power affects a 'meaning' that then causes the body to occupy space.
This is the first sentence of the argument.
3
Follow the ignorance argument
Trace the argument that beasts, sleepers, and even the most learned scholars never conceive of this 'meaning'—only the direct result of movement.
Sanusi uses a descending scale to show universality.
4
Understand the conclusion and exception
Sanusi concludes that the Mu'tazilites deny what everyone knows (movement) and affirm what no one knows (the meaning), making intentional action absurd. Note the exception for Abu al-Husayn.
The exception shows the argument targets only the later Mu'tazilites.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الرابع : إن مذهب مشايخ المعتزلة : أن قدرة العبد لا يكون لها تأثير في حصول الجسم في الحيز. وإنما تأثيرها في المعنى الذي يقتضي حصول الجسم في الحيز . إذا ثبت هذا فنقول : إن البهائم والنائمين والغافلين حين ما يتحركون ويسكنون ، لا يخطر بالهم إلا نفس حصول الجسم في الحيز . فأما إثبات معنى يقتضي حصول الجسم في الحيز . فذلك مما لا يخطر ببال أفضل الفقهاء والنحاة ، فضلاً عن العوام ، فضلاً عن الصبيان والمجانين ، فضلا عن البهائم والحشرات . وعلى هذا فنقول : الذي تصوره العقلاء وعرفوه - وهو حصول الجسم في الحيز - قد أخرجوه عن كونه مقدوراً للعبد . والذي جعلوه مقدوراً للعبد ، فهو شيء لا يتصوره أحد، ولا يدركه البتة ، لا بالقليل ولا بالكثير . ومتى كان الأمر كذلك ، فكيف يعقل أن يقال : إن الفاعل المختار أبداً ، يقصد إيجاده وتكوينه . واعلم : أن هذا الإلزام إنما يتوجه على المشايخ . أما على أبي الحسين فلا . لأنه لا يثبت هذا المعنى
Fourth: The doctrine of the Mu'tazilite masters is that the servant's power has no effect in the body's occurrence in space. Rather, its effect is in the meaning (al-ma'na) which necessitates the body's occurrence in space. If this is established, we say: Beasts, sleeping people, and the heedless, when they move and become still, nothing occurs to their minds except the very occurrence of the body in space. As for establishing a meaning that necessitates the body's occurrence in space, that is something that does not occur to the minds of the best of jurists and grammarians, let alone the common people, let alone boys and madmen, let alone beasts and insects. On this basis we say: That which rational people imagine and know—namely the body's occurrence in space—they have removed from being within the servant's power. And that which they made within the servant's power is something that no one imagines, nor perceives at all, neither a little nor much. When the matter is such, how can it be rationally said that the free agent always intends its bringing into existence and formation? And know: this objection applies only to the masters. As for Abu al-Husayn, no, because he does not affirm this meaning.
Source pages p. 89

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth argument refuting the Mu'tazilite claim that human power is effective in creating actions. The author first states the Mu'tazilite position: a servant's power does not directly make the body occupy a location (al-hayyiz) but rather affects a 'meaning' (al-ma'na) that then necessitates that occupation. The argument proceeds to show that this 'meaning' is unknown to everyone, including beasts, sleepers, the heedless, and even the most learned scholars. Ordinary people only conceive of the direct result (the body moving), not some abstract intermediary. Consequently, what is universally understood as within human power (the movement itself) is denied by the Mu'tazilites, while what they claim to be within human power is completely unknown. It is therefore absurd to say that an agent intentionally aims to create such an unknown thing. The author notes that this objection applies only to the later Mu'tazilites (al-mashayikh) and not to Abu al-Husayn al-Basri, who does not affirm this 'meaning' theory.

Key Terms
قدرة العبد
معنى
حصول الجسم في الحيز
الفاعل المختار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Mu'tazilite 'meaning' is something the agent is aware of and intends.
Correction: Sanusi argues that beasts, sleepers, scholars, and everyone else never conceive of such a 'meaning'—even the agents themselves are ignorant of it.

Checkpoint
According to the fourth argument, what do beasts and sleepers conceive when they move?
A) The supposed 'meaning' that necessitates movement
B) The very occurrence of the body in space
C) The intention of the agent
D) The power of God

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazilite 'meaning' is an unknown abstraction, making it impossible for any agent to intentionally bring it about.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Sanusi argues that we cannot intend something we do not even conceive. Think of a time you acted without fully understanding why or what you were aiming for. Does that action still count as intentional? What does this reveal about your own need for clarity before God?
?
The Mu'tazilite theory claims you directly cause a secret 'meaning' you are completely unaware of. Do you ever assume you are in control of aspects of your actions that you don't truly comprehend—like your next thought or the outcome of your effort? How does this lesson challenge your sense of self-reliance?
?
Sanusi says that what everyone knows (your visible actions) is removed from your power by the Mu'tazilites, while the hidden 'meaning' is affirmed as within your power. Have you ever experienced frustration trying to change something about yourself, only to realize you are not as in control as you thought? How can accepting this truth deepen your trust in Allah's decree?

Child's Speech and Ignorance of Means
Sanusi presents a fifth argument using a child's speech to show humans lack necessary knowledge to create their actions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fifth argument from Sanusi's Al-Matalibul Aliyah, which uses the example of a child's speech to demonstrate that humans do not have the detailed knowledge required to create their own actions.

Core Idea
If we were the creators of our actions, we would know every detail — but we don't.

In this passage, Sanusi presents a fifth argument against the view that humans create their own actions. He uses the everyday example of a child speaking, highlighting the gap between our intentional acts and our ignorance of the intricate physiological mechanics involved. This argument challenges the notion that we are the creators of our deeds.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and its translation
Start by reading the original Arabic text aloud, then follow with the English translation. This helps you appreciate the author's rhetorical style and precise terminology.
Focus on key terms like 'آلة' and 'تفاصيل'.
2
Identify the main premise
The argument begins by pointing out that a child who speaks understands the letters, but does not know how the vocal organs work to produce them. Recognize this as a claim about ignorance.
3
Trace the logical conclusion
The author concludes that if we were the creators of our actions, we would have knowledge of their details. Since we do not have such knowledge, we cannot be the creators.
This is a modus tollens argument.
4
Reflect on the broader implication
Consider how this argument fits into the larger debate on free will and divine causality. It supports the Ash'arite position that all actions are created by Allah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الخامس : إن الصبي إذا تكلم. فلا شك أنه يعقل هذه الحروف المخصوصة . وذلك لأن الكلام عبارة عن الحروف المتوالية المتعاقبة . فلو لم يكن قادراً على الإتيان بالحروف، لم يكن قادراً على الإتيان بالكلام . لكن الإتيان بكل واحد من هذه الحروف المخصوصة ، إنما يكون بآلة مخصوصة ، على كيفية مخصوصة ، ووضع تلك الآلات على وجه مخصوص ، وهي : الحلق واللسان والشفتان والأسنان وتركيب بعض هذه الأعضاء بعض . وجعل كل واحد منهما على شكل خاص ، وهيئة خاصة ، حتى يقدر الإنسان بواسطتها على الإتيان بهذه الحروف المخصوصة . لكنا نعلم أن جمهور الخلائق يتكلمون بهذه الحروف المخصوصة ، مع أنه لا يخطر ببالهم كيفية أحوال هذه الأعضاء ، اللواتي هي الآلات في إيجاد الحروف المخصوصة ، ولا يخطر ببالهم كيفية تلك الأوضاع التي باعتبارها يتمكنون من التلفظ بهذه الحروف . ثبت بمجموع ما تقدم : أن العبد لو كان موجداً لأفعال نفسه ، لكان علماً بتفاصيل تلك الأفعال ، وثبت أنه غير عالم بتلك التفاصيل ، فوجب أن لا يكون موجداً لها
Fifth: When a boy speaks, there is no doubt that he comprehends these particular letters. That is because speech consists of successive consecutive letters. For if he were not capable of producing letters, he would not be capable of producing speech. But the production of each one of these particular letters occurs only by a particular instrument, in a particular manner, and a particular placement of those instruments, namely: the throat, tongue, lips, and teeth, and the interlocking of some of these organs with others, and the making of each one of them in a specific shape and specific configuration, so that by means of them man is able to produce these particular letters. Yet we know that the majority of creatures speak these particular letters, while it does not occur to their minds the state of these organs that are the instruments in producing the particular letters, nor does it occur to their minds the manner of those placements by virtue of which they are able to utter these letters. It is established by all that preceded: If the servant were the creator of his own actions, he would have knowledge of the details of those actions. And it is established that he is not knowledgeable of those details. Therefore it is necessary that he is not their creator.
Source pages p. 89

Commentary
This passage presents the fifth argument against human creation of actions, focusing on the case of a child's speech. The author begins by noting that a child undoubtedly understands the particular letters he utters, since speech is composed of consecutive letters. If he could not produce letters, he could not produce speech. However, producing each letter requires specific vocal organs (throat, tongue, lips, teeth) arranged in a specific manner and configuration. Despite this, the vast majority of people, including the child, are completely unaware of the detailed anatomy and mechanics required for speech. They simply speak without any knowledge of the physiological processes. The argument concludes: if the servant were the creator (mujid) of his own actions, he would necessarily have knowledge of all the detailed aspects (tafasil) of those actions. Since it is established that he lacks such knowledge, he cannot be the creator.

Key Terms
الصبي
الحروف
آلة
تفاصيل الأفعال
موجد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Many think the argument means the child does not intend to speak or has no awareness of his speech.
Correction: The argument does not deny that the child intentionally speaks; it denies that the child has the detailed causal knowledge necessary to create the speech act himself.

Checkpoint
What does the argument conclude about the servant's knowledge of his actions?
A) He has complete knowledge of all details
B) He has knowledge only of the intention
C) He is not knowledgeable of the details
D) His knowledge is gained through divine revelation

Key Takeaway
The child's ignorance of speech anatomy proves that humans are not the creators of their actions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Whenever you speak, recite Surah al-Fatiha, or even move your tongue to say a single word, you are completely unaware of the intricate coordination of your throat, tongue, lips, and teeth. If you truly were the creator of that act, wouldn't you need to know every detail? How does this realization unsettle your assumption of control over your own worship? Will you still take pride in your own actions, or will you see them as gifts from Allah alone?
?
The child does not know how he forms letters, yet he speaks every day. What about you? Are there actions you perform daily—walking, thinking, even breathing—that you take for granted as your own doing? If you are ignorant of the details, how can you claim ownership? Today, before you perform your next intentional act, pause and remind yourself: 'I do not know how this happens, so it is not from me. It is from Allah.' Will you commit to that reminder?
?
This argument cuts deep: You cannot even fully account for the speech coming out of your own mouth. Now consider your sins—the harsh word, the lie, the backbite. If you cannot create your own speech, how much less can you claim to be the independent origin of that evil? Are you ready to admit that even your wrongdoings occur only by Allah's permission, and that your only role is to choose and be accountable? Will this drive you to repentance and reliance on His help to avoid sin?

The Fifth Proof: Introduction and First Aspect on Capacity Timing
Sanusi introduces the fifth proof and begins listing aspects of the servant's ignorance about his action.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the structure of the fifth proof and the first aspect that demonstrates the servant's ignorance about the timing of his capacity.

Core Idea
Ignorance of one's own action details undermines claims of independent creation.

This passage introduces the fifth proof against human independent creation. Sanusi first frames the argument: the servant is heedless of his action and agency, so he cannot be its creator. He then begins listing specific aspects of this heedlessness. The first aspect highlights a fundamental perplexity among people about when capacity is operative—a confusion that reveals deeper ignorance.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the author's claim that this proof is essentially the same as the previous one but with different aspects.
The passage cuts off mid-sentence; work with what is available.
2
Identify the overall structure
The passage has two parts: (1) a preamble stating the thesis and its relation to the previous proof, and (2) the beginning of the first aspect. Recognize the logical progression.
The first aspect is not complete, but the key point is clear: people disagree on when the capable one is capable.
3
Reflect on the first aspect's implication
Consider why this perplexity about timing indicates heedlessness. If people cannot agree on something so basic about their own capacity, how can they claim full knowledge and control over their actions?
This connects to the larger argument that such ignorance shows the servant is not the true creator.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
البرهان الخامس اعلم : أنا نبين أن العبد غافل عن أحوال فعل نفسه ، وعن أحوال فاعليته لها ، من وجوه كثيرة . ثم نبين : أنه متى كان الأمر كذلك ، امتنع كونه موجداً لتلك الأفعال . وهذا البرهان هو عين ما تقدم . إلا أن الوجوه المذكورة ههنا ، مغايرة للوجوه المذكورة في تقرير البرهان المتقدم . فنقول : الذي يدل على كون العبد غافلاً عن أحوال فعله ، وعن أحوال فاعليته ، وجوه : الأول : إن الناس تحيروا في أن القادر على الإيجاد ، أيقدر عليه حال وجود الأثر ، أو قبله ؟ فقال بعضهم : إنما يقدر عليه حال وجوده . لأنه قبل
The Fifth Proof: Know that we shall show that the servant is heedless of the states of his own action, and of the states of his agency over it, in many respects. Then we shall show: when the matter is such, his being a creator (mujid) of those actions is impossible. And this proof is essentially the same as what preceded. However, the aspects mentioned here are different from the aspects mentioned in the presentation of the preceding proof. So we say: That which indicates that the servant is heedless of the states of his action and of the states of his agency are aspects: The first: People have been perplexed about whether the one capable of bringing into existence is capable at the moment of the effect's existence, or before it? Some said: He is only capable at the moment of its existence, because before...
Source pages p. 90

Commentary
This passage begins the presentation of the fifth proof (al-burhan al-khamis). The author states that he will demonstrate that the servant is heedless (ghafil) of the states of his own action and of his agency, in many respects. From this heedlessness, the impossibility of the servant being the creator (mujid) of those actions follows. He notes that this proof is essentially the same as the preceding one, but the specific aspects (wujuh) enumerated here are different. Then he begins listing the aspects. The first aspect is a perplexity (tahayyur) among people: Is the one who is capable of bringing into existence capable at the moment the effect exists, or before it? Some say he is capable only at the moment of existence, because... The text cuts off here. The aspect is incomplete, but it reveals the line of reasoning: the servant is ignorant even of the timing of his own capacity, which further undermines claims of human creation.

Key Terms

أحوال فعل
أحوال فاعلية
قادر على الإيجاد
حال وجود الأثر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The proof denies that humans have any choice or free will.
Correction: The proof does not deny human choice; it denies that humans independently create their actions. It uses their ignorance of causal details (like the timing of capacity) as evidence that they are not the sole creators.

Key Takeaway
The first aspect of the fifth proof shows that people are confused about the basic timing of their own capacity, which indicates their heedlessness and supports the conclusion that they are not the true creators of their actions.

If you cannot fully grasp the timing of your own capacity, how can you claim to be the true creator of your deeds? This is not just a theoretical puzzle—it is a call to rely on Allah. What is one practical step you can take today to live in constant awareness that your actions come from Allah?

The Fourth Proof: Ignorance of Speech Organs
The author finalizes the fourth proof by demonstrating the servant's ignorance of speech anatomy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author uses the example of speech to demonstrate that the servant cannot be the creator of his own actions due to lack of detailed knowledge.

Core Idea
Ignorance of speech mechanics proves the servant is not the originator of his actions.

The author now brings a concrete example to finalize his fourth proof. He points to something we all do every day—speak—and shows that we do so without any conscious awareness of the intricate mechanical details. This lack of detailed knowledge, he argues, is incompatible with the claim that we create our own actions. Let's see how he builds this case.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once carefully, then read the English translation. Notice the specific anatomical terms and the logical structure.
The passage is a concluding argument, so pay attention to the conditional 'if... then...'.
2
Identify the key components of the example
The author describes the tongue, lips, teeth, their composition, and how they produce letters. Then he notes that most people speak without knowing how these organs work.
3
Analyze the logical inference
The author states a conditional: if the servant were the creator of his actions, he would have detailed knowledge. Since he lacks that knowledge, he cannot be the creator. Find this conditional in the text.
This is the core of the proof.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
The conclusion is that the servant is not the creator. Consider what this implies about human agency and divine power.
The author ends with 'وبالله التوفيق' (And with Allah is success), a standard closing.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واللسان والشفتان والأسنان وتركيب بعض هذه الأعضاء ببعض، وجعل كل واحد منهما على شكل خاص، وهيئة خاصة، حتى يقدر الإنسان بواسطتها على الإتيان بهذه الحروف المخصوصة. لكنا نعلم أن جمهور الخلائق يتكلمون بهذه الحروف المخصوصة، مع أنه لا يخطر ببالهم كيفية أحوال هذه الأعضاء، اللواتي هي الآلات في إيجاد الحروف المخصوصة، ولا يخطر ببالهم كيفية تلك الأوضاع التي باعتبارها يتمكنون من التلفظ بهذه الحروف. ثبت بمجموع ما تقدم: أن العبد لو كان موجداً لأفعال نفسه، لكان علماً بتفاصيل تلك الأفعال، وثبت أنه غير عالم بتلك التفاصيل، فوجب أن لا يكون موجداً لها. وبالله التوفيق.
And the tongue, the lips, and the teeth, and the composition of some of these limbs with others, and the making of each one of them in a specific shape and a specific form, so that a person is able by means of them to produce these specific letters. But we know that the majority of creatures speak with these specific letters, while it does not occur to their minds how the states of these limbs, which are the instruments in producing the specific letters, are, nor does it occur to their minds how those configurations by virtue of which they are able to utter these letters are. It is established by the sum of what preceded: that if the servant were the creator of his own actions, he would be knowledgeable of the details of those actions, and it is established that he is not knowledgeable of those details, so it is necessary that he is not their creator. And with Allah is the success.
Source pages p. 90

Commentary
This passage is the conclusion of the fourth proof. The author argues that the servant cannot be the creator of his own actions because he lacks detailed knowledge of how his actions are produced. The example given is speech: humans speak using the tongue, lips, and teeth, but they do not consciously think about the mechanics of these organs. If the servant were truly creating the sounds, he would need to know exactly how each organ functions to produce the specific letters. Since he does not have this knowledge, it follows that he is not the originator of his speech. The argument is summarized as a conditional: if the servant were the creator, he would know the details; but he does not, so he is not. The phrase 'وبالله التوفيق' is a closing prayer.

Key Terms
إيجاد
عبد
تفاصيل
أعضاء
حروف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that the argument denies any human agency or free will.
Correction: The argument only denies that humans have the detailed knowledge required for origination (إيجاد), not that they have no role in actions. It distinguishes between acquisition (كسب) and creation (خلق).

Checkpoint
What does the author conclude from the fact that people speak without knowing the details of their speech organs?
A) That speech is a miracle.
B) That the servant is the creator of his speech.
C) That the servant is not the creator of his speech.
D) That people are unaware of their own existence.

Key Takeaway
The lack of detailed knowledge about how we speak shows that we are not the creators of our speech, supporting the doctrine that Allah is the sole Creator.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When was the last time you truly reflected on the miracle of your tongue forming words? Does your ignorance of how you speak humble you, or do you take it for granted? How will you cultivate a daily awareness that every word you utter is a gift from your Creator?
?
The author argues that if you were the creator of your actions, you would know their every detail. Consider an action you perform repeatedly—like breathing, blinking, or walking. Why do you not know how it works? Does this not shatter any illusion of self-sufficiency? Will you pause today to thank Allah for each effortless action?
?
You have learned that the majority of people speak without knowing how their speech organs function. Are you content to remain ignorant of Allah's design in your own body? What specific step can you take this week to study one aspect of human creation, to increase your awe of the Creator?

Introduction to the Fifth Proof: The Servant's Heedlessness
The author introduces the fifth proof and distinguishes it from the fourth.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the aim of the fifth proof and how it relates to the fourth proof in showing the servant's heedlessness.

Core Idea
The fifth proof repeats the conclusion but brings new arguments against human agency.

The author now introduces the fifth proof in a series of arguments against the idea that the servant creates his own actions. He explains that this proof, while similar in outcome to the fourth, relies on a different set of observations about the servant's ignorance of his own actions. This passage sets the stage for the specific arguments to follow.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the phrase 'عين ما تقدم' – it is key to understanding the relationship.
2
Identify the two parts of the proof
The author states he will first show the servant's heedlessness, then show that this makes him unable to be the creator. Mark these two parts.
3
Understand the connection to the fourth proof
The author says this proof is exactly the same as the previous one, but the faces (arguments) are different. This means the conclusion is identical but the evidence is new.
The common misunderstanding is that it is a literal repetition.
4
Grasp the meaning of 'وجوه'
The word 'وجوه' (faces) refers to different aspects or arguments. The author will list several faces showing the servant's heedlessness. Prepare to identify them in subsequent lessons.
5
Reflect on the overall structure
This introduction sets up a proof that depends on empirical observation of human ignorance. Consider how this differs from purely rational proofs.
This proof is part of a series; each proof attacks the notion of human creation from a different angle.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
البرهان الخامس. اعلم: أنا نبين أن العبد غافل عن أحوال فعل نفسه، وعن أحوال فاعليته لها، من وجوه كثيرة. ثم نبين: أنه متى كان الأمر كذلك، امتنع كونه موجداً لتلك الأفعال. وهذا البرهان هو عين ما تقدم. إلا أن الوجوه المذكورة ههنا، مغايرة للوجوه المذكورة في تقرير البرهان المتقدم. فنقول: الذي يدل على كون العبد غافلاً عن أحوال فعله، وعن أحوال فاعليته، وجوه:

Then we show that when the matter is thus, it is impossible for him to be the creator of those actions. And this proof is exactly what preceded, except that the faces mentioned here are different from the faces mentioned in the presentation of the preceding proof. So we say: That which indicates that the servant is heedless of the states of his action, and of the states of his agency, are faces:
Source pages p. 90

Commentary
This is the introduction to the fifth proof. The author states his plan: first, to show that the servant is heedless (غافل) of the states of his own action and of his agency (فاعلية) in many respects (وجوه كثيرة). Second, to show that if such heedlessness exists, then the servant cannot be the creator (موجد) of those actions. He notes that this proof is essentially the same as the previous one (the fourth proof), but the faces (arguments) presented here are different from those in the earlier proof. This indicates that the author is providing a new set of arguments to demonstrate the same conclusion. He then transitions to listing the faces.
