The First Face: The Time of Power over Bringing into Existence
The first face: an argument from confusion about the time of power.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the argument from confusion about the time of power proves that humans lack genuine creative power.

Core Idea
Real power is self-aware; confusion proves absence of true agency.

This argument challenges the notion that humans have genuine power to create. It presents a perplexity about when power is effective—before or at the time of existence. The reasoning leads to a surprising conclusion: because people are confused, they cannot truly possess the power to bring things into existence.

Guided Path
1
Understand the perplexity
Read the passage carefully. Identify the two opposing views: is the powerful agent able to create at the time of the effect's existence, or before it?
2
Follow the reasoning
Trace the argument from the assumption of true power. Notice that if the agent is powerful in one state and not in another, he should be able to perceive the difference. This discrimination is supposed to be known by necessity.
Focus on the logical steps: power in one state → necessary awareness → no confusion expected.
3
Grasp the conclusion
Understand that since confusion does occur, the premise of true power is false. The servant does not have power over bringing into existence.
The argument uses the fact of confusion as evidence against human creative power.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأول: إن الناس تحيروا في أن القادر على الإيجاد، أيقدر عليه حال وجود الأثر، أو قبله؟ فقال بعضهم: إنما يقدر عليه حال وجوده. لأنه قبل وجوده مستمر على عدمه الأصلي، فلم يكن لقدرته فيه أثر، فيمتنع كونه قادراً عليه. لأنه لما كان في إحدى الحالتين قادراً عليه، وفي الحالة الثانية لا قدرة له عليه، والتخيير بين الحال التي فيها يقدر على الفعل، وبين الحال التي لا يقدر فيها على الفعل، يجب أن يكون مدركاً. لأن التمييز بين حال الاقتدار، وبين حال عدم الاقتدار: تمييز معلوم بالضرورة. فكان يجب أن لا يحصل هذا الالتباس. وحيث حصل: علمنا أن العبد لا قدرة له على الإيجاد.
The first: People have been perplexed as to whether the one who is powerful over bringing into existence is powerful over it at the time of the existence of the effect, or before it? So some said: He is only powerful over it at the time of its existence, because before its existence it continues upon its original non-existence, so his power had no effect in it, so it is impossible for him to be powerful over it. Because when he is in one of the two states powerful over it, and in the second state he has no power over it, the distinguishing between the state in which he is powerful over the act and the state in which he is not powerful over the act must be perceived. Because the discrimination between the state of capability and the state of lack of capability is a discrimination known by necessity. So it should have been that this confusion did not occur. And since it did occur, we know that the servant has no power over bringing into existence.
Source pages p. 91

Commentary
This is the first face (argument) of the fifth proof. The author presents a perplexity among people: is the person who is powerful (قادر) over an act of bringing into existence able to do so at the time the effect exists, or before it exists? Some argue that he is only able at the time of existence, because before that the effect is non-existent and his power has no effect. However, if the agent is powerful in one state (when the effect exists) and not powerful in the other (before existence), then the agent should be able to perceive (مدرك) the difference between these two states. This discrimination between the state of capability (اقتدار) and lack thereof is supposed to be known by necessity (بالضرورة). Therefore, there should be no confusion (التباس) on this point. The fact that people are indeed confused shows that the servant does not truly possess the power to bring into existence. The reasoning is that a real agent would have direct knowledge of his own power's scope.

Key Terms
قادر
إيجاد
أثر
اقتدار
التباس
مدرك

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument is about whether power exists at all, or that power is limited to the time of the effect's existence.
Correction: The argument is not about the existence of power per se, but about the servant's lack of direct awareness of the limits of his power. The confusion itself is evidence that the servant does not have genuine creative power, because a true agent would be self-aware of his power's scope.

Checkpoint
According to the argument, what would happen if the servant were truly powerful over bringing into existence?
A) He would be confused about the time of his power.
B) He would not need to know when he is powerful.
C) He would clearly distinguish between states of capability and incapability.
D) He would be powerful only before the effect exists.

Key Takeaway
Confusion about the time of power demonstrates that humans do not possess true creative power; a real agent would have self-awareness of his power's scope.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever been confused about whether you truly have the power to change something in your life? What does that confusion reveal about who really holds that power?
?
If you genuinely believed that all creative power belongs to Allah alone, how would your frustration over unfulfilled plans transform into trust?
?
When you claim 'I made this happen,' do you feel a sense of self-sufficiency? How does this lesson call you to abandon that illusion and acknowledge your absolute dependence on Allah?

Second Face: The Locus of the Effect
Explaining the second face: the locus of the effect of bringing into existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the confusion about the locus of creation (essence or existence) actually proves that the servant is not the true creator—because a true agent would have immediate knowledge of what he creates.

Core Idea
A true creator knows exactly what he creates; perplexity proves he is not the agent.

The second confusion addressed is about where exactly the effect of bringing into existence falls. People wonder whether it affects the essence of a thing, its existence, or both. The author argues that this very uncertainty is proof that humans are not the ones truly bringing things into existence, because a genuine agent would know by necessity what he is doing.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it, then read the English translation. Let the author's question and argument sink in.
Notice the word 'تحيروا' (they were perplexed) – this sets the tone.
2
Identify the three possible loci
The passage asks whether the effect occurs in the essence (الماهية), existence (الوجود), or both. These are the three possibilities the author presents.
These are the only logical options for where an effect could occur.
3
Understand the necessary knowledge argument
The author says that if the servant were truly performing the act of bringing into existence, he would know by necessity what he has brought into existence and effected. This immediate knowledge is a hallmark of a true agent.
Think about your own actions: you know immediately what you are doing, e.g., writing a word.
4
Connect to the larger refutation
The very fact that people are perplexed shows they are not the true creators. This is a key refutation of human agency in creating their own actions.
This argument is part of a broader debate on qadar (predestination).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وثانيها: إن الناس تحيروا في أن من أوجد شيئاً، فتأثير إيجاده، أيحصل في نفس الماهية، أو في الوجود، أو فيهما؟ ولو كان هذا الإيجاد واقعاً به، لعلم بالضرورة: أن الذي أوجده وأوقعه: ماذا؟
The second: People have been perplexed about the one who brings something into existence: does the effect of his bringing into existence occur in the essence itself, or in existence, or in both? And if this bringing into existence were actually done by him, he would know by necessity: what is that which he brought into existence and effected?
Source pages p. 91

Commentary
This is the second face. The author points to a second area of confusion: when someone brings something into existence, where exactly does the effect of his bringing into existence occur? Does it occur in the essence (الماهية) of the thing, or in its existence (الوجود), or in both? If the servant were truly the one performing this act of bringing into existence, he would know by necessity (بالضرورة) what exactly he is doing—that is, what it is that he has brought into existence and effected. The very fact that people are perplexed about this shows that they are not the true agents of bringing into existence, because a genuine agent would have direct, certain knowledge of what he is creating.

Key Terms
إيجاد
تأثير
ماهية
وجود
ضرورة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The confusion about the locus is a purely philosophical puzzle.
Correction: The author uses this confusion to demonstrate that humans are not true creators, because a true creator would have immediate knowledge.

Checkpoint

A) He would be perplexed about the locus of the effect.
B) He would know by necessity what he has brought into existence.
C) He would only know after reflection.
D) He would not care about the locus.

Key Takeaway
The perplexity about where creation occurs proves that humans are not the true agents of creation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you perform an action, do you truly know what you are doing? Consider: do you know the essence of the action, its existence, or both? Be honest: is there any perplexity in your own awareness of what you bring about? If you are uncertain about the metaphysical reality of your act, then according to this argument, you are not its true creator. What does this mean for your assumption of self-authorship? How does it change the way you approach your deeds tomorrow?

The author says a true agent knows by necessity. So when you feel uncertain or hesitant about your ability to truly originate an action, does that uncertainty itself point to a greater truth? What will you do with that truth today?
?
The perplexity about the locus of creation is a sign that humans are not the true agents. Look at your own life: Name one action today that you assumed you created yourself. The Perplexity of Effecting: Identity or Difference?
Third face: confusion over identity of effecting and effect

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophical debate about whether the attribute of effecting is identical to the effect, and how this debate reveals human ignorance of true agency.

Core Idea
Human confusion about causality proves we are not the true agents.

The author presents a third perplexity that troubled thinkers: is the attribute of 'effecting' (المؤثرية) the same as the effect itself? Two opposing views emerged, each with its own reasoning. By examining this debate, we see that even the most basic aspect of causality is unclear to humans, supporting the argument that we cannot be the true creators of our actions.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Note the two contrasting opinions and the reasoning behind each.
2
Identify the two positions
The passage gives two views: some say effecting is other than the effect, others say it is the same. Identify the evidence each side uses.
The first uses description, the second uses infinite regress.
3
Reflect on the author's purpose
Ask: Why does the author include this debate? He is not trying to solve it, but to show that humans are perplexed about something so fundamental.
This points to the servant's lack of true agency.
4
Consider the infinite regress argument
If effecting is distinct from the effect, then the effecter would require an additional connection to it, leading to an infinite chain. Understand why this is problematic.
This shows the depth of the confusion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وثالثها: إن الناس تحيروا في أن المؤثرية، هل هي نفس الأثر؟ منهم من قال: غيرها. لأن المؤثر موصوف بالمؤثرية، وغير موصوف بالأثر. فأحدهما مغاير للآخر. ومنهم من قال: بل المؤثرية نفس الأثر. إذا لو كان مغايراً له، لكان اقتضاء ذات المؤثر لتلك المؤثرية زائداً عليه. ولزم التسلسل.
The third: People have been perplexed as to whether effecting (al-mu'aththiriyya) is the same as the effect? Some of them said: It is other than it, because the effecter is described by effecting, and is not described by the effect, so one of them is different from the other. And some of them said: Rather, effecting is the same as the effect. For if it were different from it, the requirement of the essence of the effecter for that effecting would be additional to it, and there would follow an infinite regress.
Source pages p. 91

Commentary
This is the third face. The author presents a philosophical perplexity: is the attribute of effecting (المؤثرية) identical to the effect (الأثر) or distinct from it? Some argue they are distinct because the effecter is described as having effecting, not as being the effect itself. Others argue they are identical, because if they were distinct, then the effecter would require an additional connection to the effecting, leading to an infinite regress (تسلسل). The existence of this debate among people demonstrates that humans do not have direct, intuitive knowledge of the nature of causality. If the servant were the true creator, he would not be uncertain about such a fundamental aspect of his own action.

Key Terms
مؤثرية
أثر
مؤثر
اقتضاء
تسلسل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The passage aims to solve the metaphysical puzzle of whether effecting is identical to the effect.
Correction: The author is not solving the puzzle; he uses the existence of the puzzle as evidence that humans lack direct knowledge of causality and therefore cannot be the true agents.

Checkpoint
What does the debate about whether effecting is the same as the effect indicate according to the author?
A) That the servant is the true effecter.
B) That people have direct knowledge of effecting.
C) That the servant lacks direct knowledge of his own agency.
D) That infinite regress is impossible.

Key Takeaway
The very disagreement over the nature of effecting shows humans lack direct knowledge of their own agency, supporting the view that Allah alone is the true effecter.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you achieve something, do you instinctively attribute it to your own effort? How does the confusion over effecting and effect challenge your sense of self-agency?
?
If you truly believed that you are not the ultimate creator of your actions, how would that change your reaction to your successes and failures?
?
What does your own uncertainty about causality say about your need for divine guidance in understanding your relationship with your actions?

The Fourth Face: Confusion About the Locus of Knowledge
Sanusi lists possible loci and argues from confusion to non-creation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the argument that the persistence of confusion about the locus of knowledge proves that knowledge is not created by the human servant.

Core Idea
Confusion about knowledge's location disproves human creation.

In this passage, Sanusi presents the fourth face of his proof. He observes that people disagree about whether knowledge resides in the heart, brain, or rational soul. If humans were the creators of their own knowledge, they would surely know where they placed it. Since this confusion remains, it follows that knowledge is not brought into existence by the servant.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Notice how Sanusi poses the options before making his argument.
The passage begins with 'ورابعها' — this is part of a longer proof.
2
Identify the possible loci
List the different loci mentioned: heart, brain, rational soul, and even parts of the organs. This shows the extent of confusion.
3
Understand the conditional argument
Sanusi uses a conditional: if the servant created knowledge, he would know where. Since confusion persists, the antecedent is false. Trace this logic.
This is a modus tollens argument.
4
Draw the conclusion
The conclusion is explicit: 'ليس بإيجاده' — it is not due to his bringing into existence. Confirm that this directly refutes human creation of knowledge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ورابعها: إن الناس تحيروا في أن محل العلم، أهو القلب، أو الدماغ أو شيء آخر سواهما - هو النفس الناطقة؟ - وبتقدير أن كون محل العلم هو القلب أو الدماغ، فهو جميع أجزائها، أو بعض أجزائها. ولو كان موجد العلم العبد، لوجب أن يعلم أنه في أي موضع أوجده؟ وفي أي محل أحدثه؟ ولو كان الأمر كذلك، لما بقي ذلك الاشتباه. وحيث بقي، علمنا: أن حصول هذه العلوم ليس بإيجاده. فظهر: أن اختلاف الناس في هذه المطالب، يدل على أن عقول أكثر الخلق قاصرة عن حضور ماهية الإيجاد والتكوين. وإذا كان كذلك، فكيف يدعى أن جميع الحيوانات، حتى البهائم والحشرات تقصد إلى الإيجاد والتكوين والإنشاء مع أن حقيقة الإيجاد والتكوين غير متصورة.
The fourth: People have been perplexed as to whether the locus of knowledge is the heart, the brain, or something else other than them—is it the rational soul? And given that the locus of knowledge is the heart or brain, is it all of its parts or some of its parts? And if the servant were the creator of knowledge, he would necessarily know in which place he created it, and in which locus he originated it. And if the matter were thus, that confusion would not remain. And since it remains, we know that the occurrence of these knowledges is not due to his bringing into existence. So it becomes clear that the differing of people in these matters indicates that the minds of most of creation are incapable of grasping the essence of bringing into existence and formation. And if it is thus, how can it be claimed that all animals, even beasts and insects, intend to bring into existence and formation and invention, while the reality of bringing into existence and formation is not conceived?
Source pages p. 91

Commentary
This is the fourth face along with the overall conclusion of the fifth proof. The author notes that people are perplexed about the locus of knowledge: is it the heart, the brain, or the rational soul? And if it is the heart or brain, is it the whole organ or part of it? If the servant were the creator (موجد) of his own knowledge, he would necessarily know where he placed it. The fact that such confusion persists demonstrates that knowledge is not originated by the servant. The author then draws a general conclusion: the widespread disagreements in these matters show that the intellects of most creatures are incapable of grasping the essence (ماهية) of bringing into existence (إيجاد) and formation (تكوين). Therefore, it is absurd to claim that all animals, including beasts and insects, aim to perform true creation, when even humans cannot conceive of its reality.

Key Terms
محل العلم
قلب
دماغ
نفس ناطقة
إيجاد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the argument is about anatomical ignorance, but it is about the impossibility of human creation.
Correction: The point is logical: if you created it, you'd know where it is. Confusion proves you didn't create it.

Checkpoint
What does the author conclude from the fact that people disagree about the locus of knowledge?
A) That knowledge is created by the servant.
B) That the servant knows the locus of knowledge.
C) That knowledge is not created by the servant.
D) That the heart is the true locus.

Key Takeaway
The persistence of confusion about the locus of knowledge proves that humans do not create their own knowledge.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Has there ever been a time when you felt utterly certain of something, only to later discover you were mistaken? What would it mean for you to seek knowledge as a gift from Allah rather than a product of your own effort?
?

Would it make you more humble, more grateful, or more responsible? Pause and name one specific piece of knowledge you have that you must now consider a divine trust.
?
In your daily life, when you are confused about something important, do you turn to Allah for clarity, or do you rely solely on your own reasoning? This passage teaches that confusion itself is a sign that humans are not the creators of their knowledge. How will you remember that the next time you face uncertainty?

The Final Conclusion: Human Incapacity to Create
Sanusi draws a general conclusion about human incapacity and refutes animal creation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the inability to grasp the essence of creation shows that humans cannot be creators, and neither can animals.

Core Idea
True creation is beyond human and animal capacity.

Sanusi concludes his argument by widening the scope. He notes that the very fact of widespread disagreement on such matters indicates that most human minds are incapable of understanding the true nature of bringing into existence and formation. If even humans cannot conceive of creation, how can one claim that animals, including mere beasts and insects, perform acts of true origination?

Guided Path
1
Read the concluding passage
Read the Arabic and translation. Note that this follows the previous argument about the locus of knowledge.
The passage starts with 'فظهر' — indicating a conclusion.
2
Identify the main inference
The author infers from disagreement to intellectual limitation: most minds cannot grasp the essence of creation (إيجاد وتكوين).
3
Apply the reasoning to animals
The author asks a rhetorical question: if even humans cannot understand creation, how can animals be said to intend it? This is absurd.
Note the terms: بهائم (beasts) and حشرات (insects).
4
Understand the broader point
This is a general conclusion about qadar: no creature, human or animal, can be a true creator. Only Allah creates.
This supports the Ash'ari doctrine of acquisition (kasb).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ورابعها: إن الناس تحيروا في أن محل العلم، أهو القلب، أو الدماغ أو شيء آخر سواهما - هو النفس الناطقة؟ - وبتقدير أن كون محل العلم هو القلب أو الدماغ، فهو جميع أجزائها، أو بعض أجزائها. ولو كان موجد العلم العبد، لوجب أن يعلم أنه في أي موضع أوجده؟ وفي أي محل أحدثه؟ ولو كان الأمر كذلك، لما بقي ذلك الاشتباه. وحيث بقي، علمنا: أن حصول هذه العلوم ليس بإيجاده. فظهر: أن اختلاف الناس في هذه المطالب، يدل على أن عقول أكثر الخلق قاصرة عن حضور ماهية الإيجاد والتكوين. وإذا كان كذلك، فكيف يدعى أن جميع الحيوانات، حتى البهائم والحشرات تقصد إلى الإيجاد والتكوين والإنشاء مع أن حقيقة الإيجاد والتكوين غير متصورة.
The fourth: People have been perplexed as to whether the locus of knowledge is the heart, the brain, or something else other than them—is it the rational soul? And given that the locus of knowledge is the heart or brain, is it all of its parts or some of its parts? And if the servant were the creator of knowledge, he would necessarily know in which place he created it, and in which locus he originated it. And if the matter were thus, that confusion would not remain. And since it remains, we know that the occurrence of these knowledges is not due to his bringing into existence. So it becomes clear that the differing of people in these matters indicates that the minds of most of creation are incapable of grasping the essence of bringing into existence and formation. And if it is thus, how can it be claimed that all animals, even beasts and insects, intend to bring into existence and formation and invention, while the reality of bringing into existence and formation is not conceived?
Source pages p. 91

Commentary
This is the fourth face along with the overall conclusion of the fifth proof. The author notes that people are perplexed about the locus of knowledge: is it the heart, the brain, or the rational soul? And if it is the heart or brain, is it the whole organ or part of it? If the servant were the creator (موجد) of his own knowledge, he would necessarily know where he placed it. The fact that such confusion persists demonstrates that knowledge is not originated by the servant. The author then draws a general conclusion: the widespread disagreements in these matters show that the intellects of most creatures are incapable of grasping the essence (ماهية) of bringing into existence (إيجاد) and formation (تكوين). Therefore, it is absurd to claim that all animals, including beasts and insects, aim to perform true creation, when even humans cannot conceive of its reality.

Key Terms
إيجاد
تكوين
إنشاء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think animals are mere machines with no agency; the point here is stronger: they cannot perform true origination (إيجاد).
Correction: The author argues that even humans lack the power to originate, so animals certainly do not.

Checkpoint
What does the author conclude from the widespread disagreement about creation?
A) That humans and animals can create.
B) That most minds cannot grasp the essence of creation.
C) That only animals can create.
D) That creation is easily understood.

Key Takeaway
The fact that humans cannot even conceive of creation proves that no creature, including animals, can be a true creator.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

This passage says not even the most intelligent minds can grasp the essence of creation. How does that challenge your reliance on your own intellect? Will you acknowledge your limitation and turn to the only true Creator?
?
The passage uses the absurdity of attributing creation to animals to drive home the point. Is there any area in your life where you act as if you are the creator of your own success or abilities, forgetful that only Allah creates? Will you consciously affirm His sole creative power today?
?
Sanusi states that the very disagreement among people proves their incapacity. Do you find yourself disagreeing with God's decree or questioning His wisdom? How does this lesson expose the limitation of your understanding? Will you submit to His knowledge over your own?

The Servant's Power Can Only Give Existence
First part of the second argument: power only gives existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the second rational argument concludes that if the servant had power to create, it could only give existence, not form essences.

Core Idea
Power acts on existence, not on the essence itself.

This lesson unpacks the second argument against the Mu'tazilite claim that humans create their own actions. The author argues that even if we grant that a servant could bring some things into existence, that power would be limited to granting existence, not shaping essences. The argument rests on the distinction between essence and existence and the logical priority of essence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once to get the flow of the argument. Then read the translation.
Notice the compressed logical style typical of kalam.
2
Understand the essence-existence distinction
The author asserts that existence is additional to essence. Grasp why this distinction is crucial: if essence and existence were identical, power could affect essence directly.
This is a foundational premise in Islamic philosophy.
3
Follow the two-rank posteriority reasoning
The author argues that possibility is an attribute of the essence, so it is posterior to the essence. Thus, the power's effect on existence is posterior to the essence by two ranks. If power affected essence, a logical contradiction would arise.
Trace the steps: essence → possibility (attribute) → existence (effect).
4
Realize the conclusion
The argument proves that power cannot affect the essence, only existence. Therefore, if the servant had power, it would only give existence.
This sets the stage for the extension in the next lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني : إنه لو قدر العبد على إيجاد بعض الممكنات ، لما كان لقدرته تأثير ، إلا في إعطاء الوجود . لأنا بينا في سائر كتبنا بالدلائل الكثيرة : أن وجود الممكنات زائد على ماهياتها . ولا يمكن أن يكون تأثير القادر في تكوين الماهيات . فإن الشيء ما لم يكن ممكن الوجود ، لم يتصور أن يكون للقادر فيه تأثير . فتأثير القادر فيه، مسبوق بكونه في نفسه ممكن الوجود ، وكونه في نفسه ممكن الوجود يقرر ماهيته المخصوصة . لأن إمكانه صفة له ، وصفة الشيء متأخرة بالرتبة عن الموصوف . فثبت : أن تأثير القادر فيه : متأخر عن تقرر ماهيته بمرتبتين . ولو كان تأثير القادر في ماهيته ، لكان تقرر ماهيته متأخراً عن تأثير القادر فيه ، وحينئذ يلزم أن يكون المتقدم على الشيء بمرتبتين، متأخراً عنه . وذلك محال . فثبت بهذا الوجه : أن تأثير القادر، تبين في جعل الماهية ماهية ، بل ذلك التأثير في جعلها موجودة . فثبت : أن العبد لو كان قادراً على الإيجاد ، لما كان لقدرته تأثير إلا في إعطاء الوجود . واعلم : أن هذا كلام حسن قوي . إلا أنه يشم منه رائحة : أن المعدوم شيء . فثبت : أن العبد لو كان قادراً على إيجاد بعض الممكنات ، لما كان لقدرته تأثير إلا في إعطاء الوجود . فنقول : لو كان الأمر كذلك ، لوجب أن يقدر على كل الممكنات . وذلك أنه لما كان لا تأثير للموجد ، إلا في إعطاء الوجود ، وثبت : أن الوجود قضية واحدة في جميع الممكنات ، ولا تفاوت بينهما البتة في هذا المفهوم : لزم أن يكون القادر على إيجاد بعض الممكنات ، قادر على إيجاد كلها. ضرورة أن القادر على الشيء قادر على مثله
Second: If the servant were able to bring into existence some possible things, his power would have no effect except in giving existence. For we have shown in our other books with many proofs that the existence of possibles is additional to their essences. And it is not possible that the effect of the powerful one be in the formation of essences. For a thing, as long as it is not possible in existence, it is inconceivable that the powerful one have effect in it. So the effect of the powerful one in it is preceded by its being in itself possible of existence, and its being in itself possible of existence determines its specific essence. Because its possibility is an attribute of it, and the attribute of a thing is posterior in rank to the described. So it is established that the effect of the powerful one in it is posterior to the determination of its essence by two ranks. And if the effect of the powerful one were in its essence, then the determination of its essence would be posterior to the effect of the powerful one in it, and then it would be necessary that what precedes the thing by two ranks be posterior to it. And that is impossible. So by this aspect it is established that the effect of the powerful one is not in making the essence an essence, rather that effect is in making it existent. So it is established that if the servant were able to bring into existence, his power would have no effect except in giving existence. And know: This is good and strong speech, but it has a scent that the non-existent is a thing. So it is established that if the servant were able to bring into existence some possible things, his power would have no effect except in giving existence. So we say: If the matter were thus, then it would be necessary that he be able to bring into existence all possible things. And that is because since the originator has no effect except in giving existence, and it is established that existence is a single quality in all possible things, and there is no difference between them at all in this concept, it follows that the one able to bring into existence some possible things is able to bring into existence all of them, necessarily because the one able to do a thing is able to do its like.
Source pages p. 93

Commentary
This passage presents the second rational argument (al-wajh al-thani) that if the servant had power to create some possible things, his power would be limited to giving existence only, not to forming essences. The argument proceeds as follows: First, existence is additional to essences. The effect of a powerful agent cannot be upon the essence itself because the essence must already be possible in itself (determined as a specific essence) before any power can act upon it. Since possibility is an attribute of the essence, and attributes are posterior to the subject, the power's effect on a possible thing is posterior to the determination of its essence by two ranks. If power acted on the essence, the determination would be posterior to power, causing a logical contradiction (a thing's prior would be posterior). Therefore, power can only affect the existence of the essence, not its essence. Hence, if the servant could create, his power would only give existence. The author then notes that this argument has a scent of the doctrine that the non-existent (ma'dum) is a thing. Then he extends the argument: since existence is a single concept in all possibles without variation, the one able to give existence to some can give existence to all, because the able is able to the like.

Key Terms
قدرة
إيجاد
وجود
ماهية
ممكن

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the second argument, the effect of the powerful one on a possible thing cannot be on which of the following?
A) Existence
B) Essence
C) Both existence and essence
D) Neither

Key Takeaway
The effect of a powerful agent on a possible thing is only upon its existence, not its essence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You believe your actions are real, but this passage argues that power only gives existence, not essence. If you cannot create even the essence of a speck of dust, how does that humble your sense of control over your own deeds? Commit to recalculating your reliance on Allah alone for every action today.
?
The argument says that a powerful agent's effect is only on existence, not essence. In what area of your life do you act as if you are the author of your own essence, rather than receiving existence from Allah? What step will you take to surrender that illusion today?
?
If you could not even bring your own self into existence, does your gratitude reflect that reality? List one specific moment today where you will pause to acknowledge that you are merely receiving existence, not originating it.

The Uniformity of Existence and Its Implication for Divine Power
Extension of the second argument: uniformity of existence implies ability for all possibles.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why if the servant could give existence to some possibles, he would be able to give existence to all, and how this relates to the doctrine that the non-existent is a thing.

Core Idea
Existence is one and the same in all possibles, so ability for some implies ability for all.

Having established that power only gives existence, the author now extends the argument. He notes that existence is a single concept across all possible things without variation. Therefore, if a servant could give existence to some, he could give existence to all, since the able is able to its like. The author also acknowledges that this reasoning has a scent of the doctrine that the non-existent is a thing.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the author's comment about the scent of ma'dum being a thing.
The author does not endorse this doctrine; he merely notes the implication.
2
Understand the comment on ma'dum
The author says the argument has a scent that the non-existent (ma'dum) is a thing. This is a reference to the Mu'tazilite view that non-existents are entities.
The author distances himself from this view by calling it a 'scent'.
3
Follow the uniformity argument
Since existence is a single quality in all possible things without difference, the power to give existence to one implies power to give existence to all, because one is capable of the like.
This extends the conclusion from some to all possible things.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Third Argument from Mu'tazilite Doctrine
Author presents third argument based on Mu'tazilite doctrine.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Mu'tazilite view that the non-existent is a thing leads to the conclusion that if the servant could create some possible things, he could create all of them.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazilite premise that the non-existent is a thing forces a universal creative power.

This passage presents the third argument in a series refuting the idea that humans can create. The author introduces a Mu'tazilite position and uses it to derive a conclusion that is then rejected. Understanding this argument requires seeing how the Mu'tazilite premises logically entail that any ability to create one possible thing implies ability to create all.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then the translation. Notice the concise style of the argument.
The passage is dense; each phrase carries a premise.
2
Identify the Mu'tazilite premises
Find the two explicit premises: that existence is a concept common to all things, and that the powerful over a thing is powerful over its like. Also note the foundational Mu'tazilite doctrine that the non-existent is a thing.
These premises are taken from Mu'tazilite theology.
3
Trace the logical chain
Follow how the premises combine: Mu'tazilite view + universal existence + like ability lead to the conclusion that creating some possible entails creating all possible.
Consider why the conclusion is problematic for the Mu'tazilite position.
4
Recognize the dialectical purpose
Understand that the author is not endorsing this argument; rather, he is showing an implication of the Mu'tazilite view to refute it. This is a typical argumentative technique.
The author's own stance is that humans cannot create anything.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه الثالث في بيان أن العبد لو قدر على إيجاد بعض الممكنات ، لقدر على إيجاد كلها : أن نقول : مذهب مشايخ المعتزلة : أن المعدوم شيء ، وأن القادر لا تأثير له إلا في إعطاء الوجود . وثبت : أن الوجود مفهوم داخل في الكل ، وثبت : أن القادر على الشيء قادر على مثله : لزم أن يكون القادر على إيجاد بعض الممكنات قادراً على إيجاد كلها
The third aspect in explaining that if the servant were able to bring into existence some possible things, he would be able to bring into existence all of them: we say: The doctrine of the shaykhs of the Mu'tazila is that the non-existent is a thing, and that the powerful one has no effect except in giving existence. And it is established that existence is a concept included in everything, and it is established that the powerful one over a thing is powerful over its like: so it follows that the one powerful over bringing into existence some possible things is powerful over bringing into existence all of them.
Source pages p. 93

Commentary
This passage presents the third argument (al-wajh al-thalith) which relies on the Mu'tazilite school's position. The argument is concise: The Mu'tazilite shaykhs hold that the non-existent (ma'dum) is a thing (shay'), and that the powerful agent's only effect is giving existence. Since existence is a single concept that applies to all things uniformly, and since the one able to do a thing is able to do its like, it follows that if the servant could create some possible things, he would be able to create all possible things. The conclusion reinforces the same point as the previous argument: the ability to create any possible entails the ability to create all possibles, which is then refuted by subsequent arguments.

Key Terms
معدوم (ma'dum)
شيء (shay')
إعطاء الوجود (i'ta' al-wujud)
قادر (qadir)
مثله (mithluh)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The third argument is the author's own proof.
Correction: The author is citing the Mu'tazilite position dialectically; his own view is opposite.

What Mu'tazilite premise does the third argument rely on?
A) The servant has free will
B) The non-existent is a thing
C) Existence is not additional to essence
D) God creates all actions

Key Takeaway
The third argument shows that the Mu'tazilite doctrine logically entails universal creative power if any creative power is granted.

?

?

Three Proofs that the Servant Cannot Create Any Possible Thing
Three enumerative proofs with a concluding synthesis.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical reasoning behind the three proofs that a human servant cannot possess any creative power over possible things.

Core Idea
If we could create anything, we could create everything—but we can't.

This dense passage from Imam al-Razi lays out three interconnected arguments that dismantle the claim that humans can create any possible thing. The arguments build on each other to show that the very idea of partial creative power is contradictory. Let's trace each proof carefully.

Guided Path
1
Read the full passage
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the literal translation to grasp the flow of the argument.
Notice the clear structure: three 'wujuh' (aspects) followed by a conclusion.
2
Identify each proof
The passage presents three distinct proofs. Label them as: 1) opponent concession, 2) inability to create bodies, 3) inability to create life and power in oneself.
3
Understand the logical glue
Realize that the first proof is a quick refutation, the second is a necessary truth, and the third shows absurd consequences. Together they force the conclusion that if the servant could create some, he could create all.
Pay attention to the phrase 'because ability to do a thing along with inability to do its like is impossible.'
4
Connect to the conclusion
The final sentence fuses everything: 'So it is established... that he is not able to bring into existence any of them.' See how the proof chain leads to total negation of creative power.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وجوه : وأما بيان أنه يمتنع كون العبد قادراً على إيجاد كل الممكنات . فمن وجوه : الأول : إن الخصم مساعد عليه . والثاني : وهو إنا نعلم بالضرورة : أنا الآن عاجزون عن خلق الاجسام . فلو قدرنا على إيجاد بعض الممكنات لوجب أن نقدر على إيجاد الأجسام . لأن القدرة على الشيء ، مع العجز عن إيجاد مثله : محال . والثالث : وهو أنه يلزم أن نقدر على خلق القدرة والحياة لأنفسنا . وذلك محال . لأن الخلق لا يصدر إلا عن الحي القادر . ولما كان حياً ، كان قادراً ، فامتنع خلق الحياة والقدرة فيه مرة أخرى ، لامتناع اجتماع المثلين . فثبت بما ذكرنا : أن العبد لو قدر على إيجاد بعض الممكنات ، لقدر على إيجاد كلها ، وثبت : أنه لا يقدر على إيجاد كلها . فثبت : أنه لا يقدر على إيجاد شيء منها . وهو المطلوب
Aspects: As for the demonstration that it is impossible for the servant to be powerful over bringing into existence all possible things, there are aspects: First: The opponent concedes it. Second: We know by necessity that we are now incapable of creating bodies. So if we were able to bring into existence some possible things, we would have to be able to bring into existence bodies, because ability to do a thing along with inability to do its like is impossible. Third: It would entail that we are able to create power and life for ourselves. That is impossible, because creation only issues from the living, powerful. And since he is living, he is powerful, so creating life and power in him again is prevented due to the impossibility of the coincidence of two likes. So it is established by what we have mentioned that if the servant were able to bring into existence some possible things, he would be able to bring into existence all of them; and it is established that he is not able to bring into existence all of them. So it is established that he is not able to bring into existence any of them. That is the required result.
Source pages p. 94

Commentary
This passage presents three proofs (wujuh) demonstrating the impossibility of the servant having power over all possible things, which then refutes the earlier hypothetical conclusion that if he could create some, he could create all. The first proof: the opponent himself concedes this impossibility. The second proof: we know by necessity that we are currently unable to create bodies (like the sky, earth, etc.). If we could create any possible thing, we would have to be able to create bodies, because the ability to do a thing and inability to do its like is contradictory. Since we cannot create bodies, we cannot create any possible. The third proof: If the servant could create any possible, he would be able to create power and life in himself, which is impossible because only a living, powerful agent can create. Creating life and power in an already living, powerful agent would result in two likes coinciding, which is absurd. Therefore, the servant cannot create any possible thing at all. The conclusion is that the servant has no creative power whatsoever.

Key Terms
عجز (ajz)
خلق (khalq)
أجسام (ajsam)
قدرة (qudra)
حياة (hayat)
حي (hayy)
مثلان (mithlan)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: A human can create some things (e.g., actions) but not others.
Correction: The argument shows that if a human could create any possible thing, they would have to be able to create all possible things, including bodies and their own life and power. Since that is impossible, they cannot create anything at all.

Checkpoint
What is the second proof that the servant cannot have power over all possible things?
A) The opponent concedes it
B) We know by necessity we cannot create bodies
C) It would entail creating power in ourselves
D) Existence is a single concept

Key Takeaway
The servant cannot create any possible thing because if he could create any, he would have to create all, including bodies and his own life and power, which is impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever catch yourself acting as if you have the power to create your own success, solve your own problems, or bring about change in your life without fully depending on Allah? What does this lesson force you to admit about your actual inability?
?
If you truly believed that you cannot create even a single atom or the power to take your next breath, how would your reliance on Allah change in the next hour? Would your prayers, your plans, your worries look different?
?
The proof says you cannot create life or power for yourself. Where in your daily routine do you unconsciously rely on your own strength, knowledge, or will as if you generated them yourself? What would it look like to consciously attribute every moment of life and every ability to Allah alone?

The Seventh Proof: Inability to Re-Create
Author provides seventh proof: if servant cannot re-create, he cannot create initially.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the seventh proof that the servant cannot create actions, based on the impossibility of re-creation.

Core Idea
If a servant cannot re-create, he cannot create initially.

The seventh proof leverages common ground between the Ash'arites and their opponents: both agree that the servant cannot bring back an action once it ceases. The author argues that this inability logically extends to the initial creation. Let's follow the reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Let the structure of the argument sink in before dissecting it.
2
Identify the agreed premise
The proof starts with an agreed point: the servant cannot re-create actions after they cease. Note that this is common ground with the opponents.
3
Understand the analogy
The proof argues: if the servant could create initially, he could re-create, because the essence of the action is the same at both times. Essences do not change with time.
This step is the core of the argument.
4
Draw the conclusion
Since it is agreed that the servant cannot re-create, and initial creation would imply ability to re-create, it follows that the servant cannot create initially.
The conclusion is reached by modus tollens.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
البرهان السابع : الخصوم وافقونا على أن العبد لا يقدر على إيجاد أفعاله بعد عدمها . فنقول : لو كان قادراً على الإيجاد ، لكان قادراً على الإعادة . لأن الحاصل عند الإعادة : عين ما كان حاصلا عند الابتداء . وماهية الشيء لا تختلف باختلاف الأوقات . فلو كانت قدرة العبد صالحة لتكوين ذلك الشيء وقت الابتداء ، لكانت صالحة لتكوينه في وقت الإعادة . لكنا توافقنا على أن قدرة العبد غير صالحة للإعادة ، فوجب أن لا تكون صالحة للابتداء
The seventh proof: The opponents agreed with us that the servant is not able to bring into existence his actions after their non-existence. So we say: If he were able to bring into existence, he would be able to re-create. Because what is obtained at re-creation is the very same thing that was obtained at the beginning. And the essence of a thing does not differ with the difference of times. So if the servant's power were valid for forming that thing at the time of beginning, it would be valid for forming it at the time of re-creation. But we have agreed that the servant's power is not valid for re-creation, so it must not be valid for beginning.
Source pages p. 94

Commentary
This passage presents the seventh proof (al-burhan al-sabi') that the servant cannot create any action. The argument is based on a point of agreement between the Ash'ari author and his opponents (likely the Mu'tazila): both agree that the servant cannot re-create an action once it has ceased to exist (i.e., bring it back after non-existence). The proof then argues: if the servant were able to create the action initially, he would also be able to recreate it, because the thing at re-creation is the same essence as at the beginning, and essences do not change with time. Since power is the same, if it sufficed for the initial creation, it should suffice for the re-creation. But both parties agree that the servant's power is not valid for re-creation, so it is also not valid for the initial creation. Therefore, the servant cannot create any actions.

Key Terms
إعادة (i'ada)
إيجاد (ijad)
ابتداء (ibtida')
تكوين (takwin)
ماهية (mahiyya)
قدرة (qudra)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Re-creation is fundamentally different from initial creation because the object has been non-existent in between.
Correction: The argument relies on the essence (mahiyya) being the same regardless of time. The state of non-existence does not alter the essence itself; thus, if a power is capable of producing an essence at one time, it is capable at any time.

Checkpoint
What is the key point of agreement between the author and the opponents in the seventh proof?
A) The servant can create some actions
B) The servant cannot re-create actions after their non-existence
C) Power is the same for creation and re-creation
D) Essences differ with time

Key Takeaway
The seventh proof shows that inability to re-create implies inability to create, due to the sameness of essence across time.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you truly cannot create even a single action of yours, why do you still feel pride in what you accomplish? How would it change your gratitude to Allah if you internalized that even your smallest deed is from Him?
?
The proof shows that if you cannot recreate, you cannot create. Consider a sin you committed: could you bring it back? Then why do you claim ownership over it? How does this humble you?
?
This argument applies to every action you think you control. Are you willing to surrender the illusion of independent power? What would it cost you to admit that your very ability to move is a gift?

The Seventh Proof: Servant's Inability to Create
Al-Razi presents the seventh proof that the servant cannot create anything.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Al-Razi's seventh proof that the human servant cannot originally create any act, based on the logical implication from the inability to recreate.

Core Idea
If you can't recreate an act, you can't create it in the first place.

In this passage, Al-Razi presents a concise logical argument that the human being cannot be the creator of his own actions. The proof relies on a point of agreement between opposing schools: that the servant cannot recreate an act once it ceases to exist. From this admission, Al-Razi draws a powerful implication about original creation. Follow the argument step by step to see how it reaches its conclusion.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the translation to understand the overall argument.
Pay attention to the logical structure: premise, implication, reason, conclusion.
2
Identify the agreed premise
Notice that the proof starts with a statement of agreement: both sides agree the servant cannot recreate an act after it ceases to exist.
This is the foundation of the argument.
3
Follow the implication chain
Trace the reasoning: if the servant could originally create, he could recreate (because essence is the same). Since he cannot recreate, he cannot originally create.
The key is the identity of essence across time.
4
Reflect on the common misunderstanding
The passage does not assume recreation is impossible; it uses the opponent's own admission to prove original creation is also impossible.
Avoid the misconception that the proof is circular.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
البرهان السابع
الخصوم وافقونا على أن العبد لا يقدر على إيجاد أفعاله بعد عدمها فنقول : لو كان قادراً على الإيجاد ، لكان قادراً على الإعادة . لأن الحاصل عند الإعادة : عين ما كان حاصلا عند الابتداء . وماهية الشيء لا تختلف باختلاف الأوقات . فلو كانت قدرة العبد صالحة لتكوين ذلك الشيء وقت الابتداء ، لكانت صالحة لتكوينه في وقت الإعادة. لكنا توافقنا على أن قدرة العبد غير صالحة للإعادة ، فوجب أن لا تكون صالحة للابتداء
The Seventh Proof: The opponents agree with us that the servant is not capable of bringing his acts into existence after their non-existence. So we say: If he were capable of bringing into existence (creation), he would be capable of recreation. Because what is obtained at recreation is the very same thing that was obtained at the beginning. And the essence of a thing does not differ with the difference of times. So if the servant's power were valid for the origination of that thing at the time of beginning, it would be valid for its origination at the time of recreation. But we agree that the servant's power is not valid for recreation, so it must be that it is not valid for beginning.
Source pages p. 94

Commentary
This passage presents the seventh proof that the servant (human being) cannot create any act. The author begins by stating a point of agreement with the opponents: the servant cannot bring his acts into existence after they have ceased to exist (i.e., cannot recreate them). The argument then proceeds: if the servant were capable of original creation (bringing something into existence from nothing), he would also be capable of recreation. The reason given is that the thing recreated is identical in essence to the thing originally created; essence does not change over time. Therefore, the same power that suffices for original creation should suffice for recreation. Since it is agreed that the servant lacks the power for recreation, it follows that he must also lack the power for original creation. This is a logical implication based on the identity of the act's essence across time.

Key Terms
قدرة
إيجاد
إعادة
ابتداء
ماهية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the proof assumes recreation is impossible for the servant, making it circular or arbitrary.
Correction: The proof does not assume recreation is impossible; it uses the opponent's own admission that recreation is impossible to argue that original creation must also be impossible. The reasoning is valid because of the identity of essence.

Checkpoint
According to the seventh proof, why does the servant's inability to recreate imply inability to originally create?
A) Because the original act is different from the recreated act.
B) Because the essence of the thing is the same in both original creation and recreation.
C) Because the opponents deny recreation is possible for God.
D) Because power is indivisible and cannot be used for two acts.

Key Takeaway
Al-Razi shows that the servant cannot originally create acts because if he could, he would also be able to recreate them—which both sides agree he cannot.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have never been able to recreate a single act of yours—not even a word you've spoken. Does this proof force you to admit that even your 'original' actions are not truly under your power? If so, will you today stop claiming ownership of your deeds and instead attribute every movement to Allah, asking Him to make you a vessel of righteousness?
?
When you sin, you often say 'I did it myself.' But if you cannot even recreate a good deed, how can you claim full authorship of a sin? Can you honestly say you created that sin independently? How does this realization change the way you repent—will you now beg Allah not only for forgiveness but also for the power to have done otherwise?
?
This proof shows that your inability to recreate proves your inability to create originally. What act will you perform today—prayer, charity, or even a kind word—that you will consciously acknowledge as being entirely created by Allah through you? Write one specific act and commit to saying before it: 'This is from You, not from me.'

Why the Servant Cannot Recreate His Own Act
Objection to seventh proof and its detailed refutation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical arguments against the possibility of a servant recreating his own act, and how this refutes the objection to the seventh proof.

Core Idea
Recreation by the servant is impossible because it would require either an overburdened power or shared power, both impossible.

This passage presents a sophisticated objection to the proof that the Creator of the world must be capable of recreation. The objector raises two challenges: first, that recreation of the non-existent is impossible, and second, that even if possible, the power for original creation does not guarantee power for recreation. The author then provides a detailed refutation, showing why the servant—unlike God—cannot recreate his own acts.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the first part of the passage (up to 'مانعا في الطرف الآخر') to understand the two challenges raised by the objector.
The first challenge is about the impossibility of restoring something that has completely perished. The second is about the non-necessity of ability to recreate from ability to create originally.
2
Identify the two divisions in the answer
Notice that after 'ثم نقول', the author divides the possibility of recreation by the servant into two exclusive options: either by the same power used originally, or by another power.
This is a classic logical division (taqsīm) to show both paths lead to impossibility.
3
Examine the 'same power' argument
Read the argument that the same power cannot be used because it would have to be directed to two objects at the same time, leading to infinite regress.
The key point: a finite power can only be directed to one object at a time.
4
Examine the 'other power' argument and conclusion
Read the argument that a different power cannot be used because two powers cannot produce one act. Then note the conclusion that the servant cannot recreate, and why this does not apply to original creation.
The conclusion reinforces the original proof by showing that the servant's inability is due to specific logical constraints that do not affect God.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : لا نسلم أن إعادة المعدوم جائزة في الجملة . سواء كان ذلك يمتنع في حق الله تعالى ، أو في حق العبد . وبيانه : أن الذي عدم فقد فني ولم يبق منه لا ذاته ولا حكم من أحكامه . فإذا حصل بعد ذلك شيء آخر ، فهو مغاير للأول ، وامتنع أن يكون عين ذلك الذي عدم . سلمنا : أن الإعادة في الجملة جائزة . لكن لم قلتم : إن القادر على الابتداء ، يجب أن يكون قادراً على الإعادة ؟ وتقريره : إن الابتداء يمتاز عن الإعادة بأمر من الأمور . فلم لا يجوز أن يقال : إن ما به حصل الامتياز يكون شرطاً في أحد الطرفين أو مانعا في الطرف الآخر ؟ ثم نقول : الفرق بين الإعادة وبين الابتداء . هو أنه لو صحت الإعادة من العبد ، لكان إما أن يقدر على إعادته بتلك القدرة ، التي بها أوجد ذلك الفعل في الابتداء ، أو بغير تلك القدرة . والقسمان باطلان . أما أنه إعادة ذلك الفعل بعين القدرة التي بها أوجده في الابتداء . فذلك لأن القدرة لا بد وأن تتعلق في كل وقت بإيجاد فرد من أفراد ذلك النوع . فلو تعلقت أيضا في وقت من الأوقات بإعادة ما عدم ، لكان قد تعلقت في الوقت الواحد ، في المحل الواحد من الجنس الواحد بأكثر من مقدور واحد ولو جاز ذلك لما كان عدد أولى من عدد . فيلزم جواز تعلقها بما لا نهاية له . وذلك محال . لأنه يلزم أن لا يبقى التفاوت بين القادر وبين الأقدر . فثبت : أن إعادة الفعل بعين القدرة التي حصل بها الإيجاد في الابتداء : محال . وأما أنه لا يمكن إعادته بقدرة أخرى. لأنه لو جاز أن تتعلق قدرتان بإيجاد مقدور واحد ، لجاز قيام كل واحد منهما بقادر آخر . وذلك يفضي إلى حصول مقدور ، بين قادرين. وهو محال . فثبت : أن العبد لو قدر على إعادة فعل نفسه ، لقدر عليه . إما بعين تلك القدرة وإما بقدرة أخرى وثبت أن كل واحد منهما محال فلزم القول : بأن العبد لا يقدر على الاعادة . وهذا العذر غير موجود في القدرة على إيجاد الفعل ابتداء . فثبت : أنه لا يلزم من قدرة العبد على الإيجاد ابتداء ، قدرته على إعادة ذلك الفعل . فهذا هو تقرير هذا الفرق .
If it is said: We do not concede that the recreation of the non-existent is permissible in general, whether that is impossible with respect to God the Exalted or with respect to the servant. Its explanation: that which has ceased to exist has perished and nothing remains of it, neither its essence nor any of its properties. So if something else occurs after that, it is different from the first, and it is impossible that it be the very same thing that ceased to exist. We concede: that recreation in general is permissible. But why do you say that the one capable of original creation must be capable of recreation? Its explication: original creation is distinguished from recreation by some matter. So why is it not permissible to say that that by which distinction occurs is a condition in one of the two sides or an obstacle in the other side? Then we say: The difference between recreation and original creation is that if recreation from the servant were valid, it would either be that he is capable of its recreation by that same power by which he brought that act into existence in the original creation, or by another power. Both divisions are invalid. As for the recreation of that act by the very same power by which he brought it into existence in the original creation, that is because power must in every time be directed to bringing into existence an individual of the individuals of that type. So if it were also directed at some time to recreating what has ceased to exist, it would have been directed in one time, in one substrate of the same type, to more than one object of power; and if that were permissible, then no number would be more appropriate than another number, so it would necessitate the permissibility of its being directed to infinitely many. That is impossible, because it would necessitate that there be no difference between the powerful and the more powerful. So it is established that the recreation of the act by the very power by which the original creation occurred is impossible. As for that it cannot be recreated by another power, because if it were permissible that two powers be directed to bringing into existence one object of power, it would be permissible for each of them to subsist in another powerful being, and that would lead to the occurrence of an object of power between two powerful beings, which is impossible. So it is established that if the servant were capable of recreating his own act, he would be capable of it either by that same power or by another power, and it is established that each of the two is impossible, so it is necessary to say that the servant is not capable of recreation. And this excuse does not exist in the power to bring the act into existence originally. So it is established that it does not follow from the servant's power to bring into existence originally his power to recreate that act. This is the elaboration of this distinction.
Source pages p. 95

Commentary
This passage presents an objection to the seventh proof and its detailed answer. The objector first raises two points: (1) recreation of the non-existent is not permissible at all, because what has perished is gone entirely; (2) even if recreation is permissible, why must the ability to originally create imply ability to recreate, since there might be a distinguishing condition or obstacle? The author then provides a thorough rebuttal. He concedes recreation is possible in general but then addresses the core of the issue. He argues that if the servant were to recreate his own act, it would have to be either with the same power used originally or with a different power. The same power cannot be used because at any given time a power is directed to a specific individual of a type; if it were also directed to the recreation, it would be directed to two objects at once, leading to infinite regress—a logical impossibility. A different power cannot be used because two powers cannot produce one act; that would make one object of power dependent on two agents, also impossible. Since both possibilities are impossible, recreation by the servant is impossible. However, this does not affect the original creation scenario because the constraints of multiple objects or multiple powers do not apply there. Therefore, the objection fails to undermine the original proof.

Key Terms
إعادة
ابتداء
قدرة
مقدور
امتياز

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Recreation of the non-existent is absolutely impossible, for both God and the servant.
Correction: The author concedes that recreation is possible for God, but proves it is impossible for the servant due to logical constraints on finite power.

Checkpoint
According to the answer, why cannot the servant recreate his act by the same power used in original creation?
A) Because that power would be too weak for recreation.
B) Because that power would have to be directed to two objects at the same time, leading to infinite regress.
C) Because the original act is of a different type than the recreation.
D) Because God alone can recreate.

Key Takeaway
The servant's power is limited such that he cannot recreate his own act, because it would require either directing one power to multiple objects or sharing an object between two powers, both impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you rely on your own ability to repeat a good deed you once performed, do you assume that power is entirely yours? How does this lesson's proof that you cannot 'recreate' an act challenge that assumption?
?
If every act you produce is a new original creation by God's power, not your own recreation, how should that change your gratitude and humility after each act of obedience?
?
The proof argues that your power cannot handle two objects at once. In what areas of your life have you been acting as if your power is unlimited, trying to direct it to multiple goals? What must you entrust to God instead?

Refuting the Corrupt Analogy and Establishing I'ada
Author refutes the analogy and establishes the permissibility of recreation via necessary concomitants.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the author declares the analogy corrupt and how he defends the permissibility of recreation (i'ada) through the principle of necessary concomitants of the essence.

Core Idea
The analogy is corrupt because it oscillates; i'ada is permissible as a necessary concomitant.

The author engages with an opponent's analogy that denies the servant's ability to recreate his action. He first exposes the logical flaw in the analogy, showing it either fails to establish a distinction or begs the question. Then he provides a positive argument based on a fundamental kalam principle: once something is possible in one state, it remains possible in all states.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the translation to grasp the flow of the argument.
Notice the two main parts: refutation of analogy and positive proof.
2
Identify the corrupt analogy
Find where the author says 'This analogy is corrupt' and understand why it oscillates between two options.
The analogy fails either due to a valid distinction or due to an invalid one.
3
Understand the assumption refutation
The opponent assumes the servant cannot recreate; the author rejects that assumption, causing the proof to collapse.
4
Learn the positive proof for i'ada
The author argues that permissibility of existence is a necessary concomitant of essence, so if i'ada is possible in one state, it is always possible.
This principle is foundational in kalam theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم نقول : هذا الذي ذكرتم قياس فاسد . لأن الفرق الذي ذكرناه . إن صح ، فقد بطل الجمع . وإن فسد ، منعنا الحكم في الأصل ، وقلنا : العبد يقدر على الإعادة . فهذا القياس دائر بين ظهور الفارق بين الأصل والفرع. وبين منع الحكم في الأصل ثم نقول : إنكم بنيتم هذه الحجة على أنا سلمنا أن العبد لا يقدر على إعادة فعل نفسه . ولو منعنا ذلك ، وحكمنا بأنه يجوز منه إعادة فعله ، فحينئذ لا يبقى دليلكم البتة والجواب : أما بيان أن الإعادة جائزة فهو أن جواز الوجود من لوازم الماهية ، فلما ثبت جواز إيجاده في بعض الأحوال ، وجب أن يدوم ذلك في كل الأحوال . قوله : ( إنه نفي محض ، فكيف يمكن الحكم عليه بهذا الحكم المخصوص ) ؟ قلنا : قولكم : بأنه لا يصح الحكم عليه : حكم عليه بامتناع الحكم عليه. ولما كان عدمه غير مانع من هذا الحكم ، فلم لا يجوز أن أيضاً من الحكم الذي ذكرناه ؟ قوله : ( الإعادة ممتازة عن الابتداء، بأمر ما فلم لا يجوز أن يكون ما به الامتياز ، شرطاً في أحد الطرفين ، وإلا لكان ذلك الوصف أيضاً مبتدءاً ، ويلزم التسلسل . وإذا كان ذلك الوصف عدماً محضاً ، امتنع دخوله في المقتضى لا يمنع وأما الفرق الذي ذكروه فهو بناء على أصولهم الفاسدة في أنه يمتنع حصول مقدور واحد ، بين قادرين. وفي أن القدرة الواحدة في الوقت الواحد ، في المحل الواحد ، من الجنس الواحد ، لا تتعلق إلا بمقدور واحد . وكل هذه الأصول عندنا فاسدة . قوله : ( لو ثبت فساد هذا الفرق ، منعنا الحكم وقلنا : العبد يقدر على الإعادة قلنا : هذا لا سبيل إليه لأن الأمة مجمعة على أن العبد لا يقدر على إعادة فعل نفسه، فالقول بأنه يقدر على هذه الإعادة يكون خرقاً للإجماع . وبالله التوفيق
Then we say: This analogy you have mentioned is corrupt. Because the difference we mentioned, if it is valid, then the combination is invalid. And if it is invalid, we deny the ruling in the original and say: The servant is able to recreate. So this analogy oscillates between the appearance of a difference between the original and the branch, and between denying the ruling in the original. Then we say: You have built this argument on the assumption that we concede that the servant is not able to recreate his own action. But if we deny that and rule that he is permitted to recreate his action, then your proof remains nothing at all. The answer: As for the demonstration that recreation is permissible, it is that the permissibility of existence is among the necessary concomitants of the essence. So when the permissibility of bringing it into existence in some states is established, it must continue in all states. His statement: "It is a pure negation, so how can one judge it with this specific judgment?" We say: Your statement that it is not valid to judge it is a judgment upon it of the impossibility of judging it. And since its non-existence is not an obstacle to this judgment, why is it not also permitted from the judgment we mentioned? His statement: "Recreation is distinguished from origination by something. So why is it not permissible that that by which the distinction occurs is a condition in one of the two sides? Otherwise that attribute would also be originated, and regress would follow. And if that attribute is a pure negation, its inclusion in the causative factor is impossible." That does not prevent. As for the difference they mentioned, it is built upon their corrupt principles: that it is impossible for a single object of power to occur between two powerful agents; and that a single power at a single time, in a single locus, of a single genus, relates only to a single object of power. All these principles are corrupt according to us. His statement: "If the corruption of this difference is established, then we deny the ruling and say: The servant is able to recreate." We say: There is no way to that because the community is unanimous that the servant is not able to recreate his own action, so saying that he is able to recreate that would be a breach of consensus. And with God is success.
Source pages p. 96

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of an analogy (qiyas) used to deny the servant's ability to recreate his own action. The author first labels the analogy as corrupt (fāsid). He explains that the analogy depends on either a valid distinction between the original and the branch, in which case the combination is invalid, or an invalid distinction, in which case the ruling in the original case is rejected and the servant is declared able to recreate. The argument thus oscillates. Then the author notes that the opponent built the argument on the assumption that the servant cannot recreate his action; if that assumption is rejected, the proof collapses. To prove recreation is permissible, the author invokes the principle that the permissibility of existence is a necessary concomitant of the essence (jawāz al-wujūd min lawāzim al-māhiyya), so once permissibility is established in one state, it continues in all states. He then addresses an objection: how can a pure negation (nafī maḥḍ) be subject to a positive judgment? The response is that the objection itself judges the negation by declaring it impossible to judge, so the negation is not immune to judgment. Another objection is that recreation is distinguished from origination by something; if that something is a condition in one case, it would lead to infinite regress, and if it is a pure negation, it cannot be a cause. The author replies that this does not prevent the judgment. Next, he dismisses the difference (farq) the opponents rely on as based on corrupt principles: that a single object of power cannot occur between two agents, and that a single power relates only to a single object of power. These principles are rejected. Finally, the opponent argues that even if the difference is corrupt, the servant cannot recreate due to consensus; the author responds that consensus indeed establishes the servant cannot recreate his own action, so claiming otherwise breaches consensus. The passage ends with a prayer.

Key Terms
قياس فاسد
إعادة
جواز الوجود
لوازم الماهية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The opponent assumes that the servant cannot recreate his action is a conceded premise.
Correction: The author denies that premise; if it is rejected, the opponent's proof collapses.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what is the basis for the permissibility of recreation (i'ada)?
A) The consensus of the community
B) The principle that permissibility of existence is a necessary concomitant of the essence
C) The rejection of the analogy
D) The ability of the servant

Key Takeaway
The permissibility of recreation rests on the principle that what is possible in one state remains possible in all states, as a necessary concomitant of the essence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In this lesson, the author points out that the opponent's argument assumes something without justification. Are you holding any assumptions about your own limitations—in your faith, character, or ability to change—that you have never truly examined? Will you challenge at least one such assumption today with the same critical spirit?
?
The principle here is that if something is possible in one state, it must be possible in all states. Is there an area of your life where you have given up on change, thinking 'that was possible then, but not now'? How does this lesson compel you to reconsider that?
?
The author argues that the permissibility of existence is a necessary concomitant of the essence—it doesn't change. Reflect: Do you treat Allah's mercy and forgiveness as something that can be possible at some times but not others? What would change in your relationship with Him if you truly believed that His mercy is always possible, not contingent on your current state?
