Answering Objections and Rejecting Opponent's Principles
Author answers objections and refutes opponent's principles and consensus claim.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author responds to objections about judging pure negations and the distinction between recreation and origination, and how he rejects the opponent's principles and consensus argument.

Core Idea
Even a pure negation can be judged; the distinction between i'ada and ibtida' does not preclude i'ada's permissibility.

The author now faces two objections: first, that a pure negation cannot be subject to a positive judgment, and second, that recreation is distinguished from origination by something that would lead to regress. He also counters the opponent's underlying principles (one object of power cannot be between two agents, etc.) and addresses the consensus argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to follow the series of objections and responses.
Notice the structure: objection, response; then another objection, response; then dismissal of principles and final consensus point.
2
Analyze the first objection and response
The objector says a pure negation cannot be judged; the author replies that the objection itself is a judgment.
This is a clever refutation by self-reference.
3
Analyze the second objection and response
The objector argues that recreation is distinguished from origination by something; if that something is a condition, it leads to regress; if it is a pure negation, it cannot be a cause. The author says this does not prevent the judgment.
The author does not elaborate further here.
4
Understand the rejection of principles and consensus
The author dismisses the opponent's principles (e.g., one object of power cannot be between two agents) as corrupt. He then acknowledges the consensus that the servant cannot recreate, but notes that this consensus is the ruling, not a proof against permissibility.

?

Are there areas in your faith where you have accepted a common distinction as definitive, without asking whether it truly bars the possibility? ?
The author acknowledges the consensus that the servant does not recreate his action, yet does not treat that as a proof of impossibility. Are there aspects of your relationship with Allah where you have mistaken 'I have never experienced this' for 'this cannot be'? Commit yourself today to distinguish between what is actually impossible and what you have simply not seen.

The Eighth Proof: Servant Cannot Create Motion
Author presents a proof that the servant is not the creator of his action.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the eighth proof uses a dilemma and atomic theory of motion to show that the servant cannot be the creator of his action.

Core Idea
The servant cannot create his action because creation requires detailed knowledge of motion's atomic structure.

This lesson walks through the eighth proof from al-Matālib al-ʿĀliya. The proof presents a dilemma: if the servant creates his action, he must intend it either with or without a specific time. Both options lead to impossibility. The argument relies on the nature of motion as composed of indivisible moments and loci. By the end, you'll see why the servant cannot be the creator.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
The proof is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the dilemma structure
Notice that the proof presents two possibilities: either the servant intends the action's existence without specifying a time, or he intends it in a specific time. These are the two horns of the dilemma.
The author argues both are impossible.
3
Analyze the first option
Focus on why intending absolute existence leads to absurdity: the action would either occur at all times or never. Understand why that is impossible.
This refutes the first horn.
4
Analyze the second option
Now examine the second horn: intending the action in a specific time. The author breaks down motion into indivisible occurrences and loci, arguing that intending such motion requires detailed knowledge that most people lack.
This refutes the second horn, establishing the proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
البرهان الثامن لو كان العبد موجداً لفعله . لكان إما أن يقصد إيجاده فقط ، أو يقصد إيجاده في الوقت المعين. والأول باطل . لأنه لو قصد مطلق الإيجاد من غير أن يقيد ذلك الإيجاد بوقت معين ، لم يكن وقوع ذلك الفعل في بعض الأوقات أولى من وقوعه في الوقت الآخر. فيفضي إلى أن يحصل حدوثه في كل الأوقات ، أو إلى أن لا يقع حدوثه في شيء من الأوقات . والكل محال . وأما الثاني : وهو أن يقال : إنه قصد إلى إيقاع الفعل في الوقت المعين . فهذا أيضاً باطل . لأن الحركة من أول المسافة إلى آخرها ، عبارة عن حصولات متوالية في أحياز متعاقبة . وكل واحد من تلك الحصولات غير منقسم . فإذا كان لا ماهية للحركة إلا حصولات غير منقسمة ، متوالية في أحياز متلاصقة ، غير منقسمة ، كان القصد إلى إيجاد تلك الحركة : قصداً إلى إيجاد تلك الحصولات الغير منقسمة في تلك الأحياز الغير منقسمة . والقصد إلى الشيء بدون العلم بماهية المقصود إليه : محال . فوجب أن يكون القاصد إلى تكوين الحركة عالماً بالضرورة بأنه يحدث ويدخل في الوجود حصولات متعاقبة غير منقسمة في أحياز متلاصقة غير منقسمة . ومعلوم أن هذا العلم : مقصود في حق الأكثرين فثبت : أن موجد الحركة ومكونها ، ليس هو العبد
The eighth proof: If the servant were the creator of his action, he would either intend its existence alone, or intend its existence in a specific time. The first is invalid, because if he intended absolute existence without restricting that existence to a specific time, then the occurrence of that action at some times would not be more likely than its occurrence at other times, leading to its occurrence at all times or to its non-occurrence at any time—both are impossible. As for the second, namely that he intended to bring about the action in a specific time, this too is invalid, because motion from the beginning of the distance to its end consists of successive occurrences (ḥuṣūlāt) in consecutive loci (aḥyāz). Each of those occurrences is indivisible. Since the essence of motion is nothing but indivisible occurrences, successive in contiguous indivisible loci, then intending to bring about that motion is intending to bring about those indivisible occurrences in those indivisible loci. Intending something without knowledge of the essence of the intended is impossible. Therefore, the one who intends to generate motion must necessarily know that he brings into existence successive indivisible occurrences in contiguous indivisible loci. It is well known that this knowledge is aimed at by most people? So it is established that the originator and generator of motion is not the servant.
Source pages p. 97

Commentary
This passage presents the eighth proof (al-burhān al-thāmin) against the claim that the servant is the creator of his own action. The proof is a dilemma. If the servant were the creator, then either he intends the absolute existence of the action without specifying a time, or he intends its existence in a specific time. The first option leads to absurdity: if no time is specified, then the action is equally likely at any time, resulting either in it occurring at all times or never—both absurd. The second option is also impossible because motion is composed of indivisible occurrences (ḥuṣūlāt) in indivisible loci (aḥyāz). To intend to create such a motion, one must have knowledge of those indivisible parts and their precise loci. But most people lack such detailed knowledge, so they cannot be the creators. Hence, the mover and generator of motion is not the servant. The proof relies on the atomic theory of motion (indivisible parts) and the principle that intention requires knowledge of the intended essence.

Key Terms
البرهان الثامن
موجد
قصد
إيجاد
وقت معين
حركة
حصولات غير منقسمة
أحياز متلاصقة
علم ضروري

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Many think that intending an action in a specific time is simple and straightforward.
Correction: The author shows that such intention requires detailed knowledge of the atomic structure of motion, which most people do not possess.

Checkpoint
What is the first possibility in the eighth proof's dilemma?
A) The servant intends his action in a specific time
B) The servant intends only the existence of his action without time specification
C) The servant intends both existence and time together
D) The servant does not intend at all

Key Takeaway
The eighth proof establishes that human beings lack the detailed knowledge of motion's atomic structure required to be the creators of their own actions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever truly felt that your actions are not entirely your own, that there is a detailed knowledge and power beyond you that brings them into existence? How does that change how you approach your intentions and efforts today?
?
If you lack the knowledge of the atomic structure of your own motions, how can you claim to be the independent cause of your deeds? Does this realization humble you enough to acknowledge your absolute dependence on Allah in every movement?
?
When you sin, do you attribute the action solely to yourself, forgetting that even the ability to move is a creation of Allah? How can this proof transform your repentance and reliance on His mercy?

The Ninth Proof: Effect of Power and Its Locus
Author begins a proof on the effect of the servant's power.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the structure of the ninth proof regarding the effect of the servant's power and the two logical possibilities it examines.

Core Idea
The servant's power either leaves the action from its locus or it does not.

This lesson introduces the ninth proof (al-burhān al-tāsiʿ) on the relationship between human power (qudra) and the actions it supposedly effects. The author begins with a conditional statement that sets up two mutually exclusive possibilities. Understanding this distinction is key to following the proof's subsequent elimination of each option, ultimately showing that human power cannot truly cause actions.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic text once, then the literal translation. Note that the proof is introduced as 'the ninth proof' and begins with a conditional 'if... then' structure.
The passage is short but foundational for the argument that follows.
2
Identify the two alternatives
The passage presents two possibilities for how the effect of the servant's power could occur: either by 'leaving it from the locus of that power' or 'not by leaving it from its locus.' Mark these in the text.
These alternatives are exhaustive and mutually exclusive.
3
Reflect on the significance of 'locus'
Consider what 'locus of power' (maḥall al-qudra) means. In kalam, power inheres in the servant, and the action supposedly originates from that power. The proof questions whether the effect occurs by the action leaving that locus.
This concept is key to understanding the Ash'ari position on acquisition (kasb).
4
Prepare for the proof's next steps
The proof will now examine each possibility and show that both lead to impossibility, thereby refuting the claim that human power truly effects actions. This lesson sets the stage for that logical elimination.
You may need to refer to further lessons to see how the proof concludes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
البرهان التاسع لو أثرت قدرة العبد في حدوث الفعل . لكان أثرها في حدوث ذلك الفعل ، إما أن يكون بتركه من محل تلك القدرة ، أو لا بتركه من محلها .
The ninth proof: If the servant's power were to effect the occurrence of the action, then its effect on the occurrence of that action would either be by its leaving it from the locus of that power, or not by its leaving it from its locus.
Source pages p. 97

Commentary
This passage introduces the ninth proof (al-burhān al-tāsiʿ) regarding the effect of the servant's power (qudra). The proof begins with a conditional statement: if the servant's power were to effect the occurrence of the action, then its effect would be one of two possibilities: either the effect occurs by the power leaving the action from its locus (i.e., the action is detached from the power's locus), or it occurs not by leaving it from its locus. The text then breaks off, but the structure suggests the author will examine each possibility and show that both lead to impossibility, thus proving that the servant's power does not truly effect the action. This is a classic kalam argument about the relationship between power (qudra) and the locus of its effect.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that the proof assumes the effect must necessarily be by leaving the locus, but it actually considers both possibilities.
Correction: The passage explicitly presents both alternatives: 'by leaving it' and 'not by leaving it.' Both are examined equally.

Checkpoint

A) It either occurs with knowledge or without knowledge
B) It either occurs by leaving the action from the power's locus or not by leaving it
C) It either occurs in a specific time or at all times
D) It either is a pure negation or an affirmation

Key Takeaway
The ninth proof frames the debate over human power by presenting two ways the effect could occur, both of which will be argued to be impossible.

The Ninth Proof: The Servant's Power is Not an Agent
This passage presents the ninth proof that the servant's power cannot be the agent causing the action, dividing the possibility into two impossible cases: with or without the substrate.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational proof that the servant's power cannot be the agent causing an action, and why both possible scenarios (with or without the substrate) are impossible.

Core Idea
The servant's power cannot be an agent because it is contingent on a receptive substrate.

This passage presents the ninth proof from al-Matalib al-Aliyah that the servant's power is not the agent of the action. The argument divides the possibility into two exhaustive cases: either the power's effect occurs together with its substrate (the bodily locus) or without it. Both are shown to be impossible due to logical contradictions. Understanding this proof is key to the Ash'ari position on human action.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the two divisions.
Carefully read the Arabic passage and its translation. Note that the author states two possibilities: effect with the substrate or without it.
These two divisions cover all possibilities.
2
Understand why effect with substrate is impossible.
The substrate is merely receptive (possible) while the power is creative (necessary). Making the receiver a part of the creator is contradictory and impossible.
This is a key logical point: possibility contradicts necessity.
3
Understand why effect without substrate is impossible.
If the power were independent in its effect, it would also be independent in its existence, but it exists only in the substrate.
Independence in effect implies independence in existence.
4
See how both divisions lead to the conclusion.
Since both cases are false, the original assumption that the power is effective is false. Hence, the servant's power is not the agent.
This is a proof by exhaustive case analysis.
5
Note the broader implication.
The proof concludes that corporeal powers are not effective in existence at all, a point attributed to the philosophers.
This aligns with the Ash'ari denial of causal efficacy in created agents.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
البرهان التاسع
وذلك هو المطلوب
لو أثرت قدرة العبد في حدوث الفعل . لكان أثرها في حدوث ذلك الفعل ، إما أن يكون بتركه من محل تلك القدرة ، أو لا بتركه من محلها .
والقسمان باطلان ، فالقول بأن قدرة العبد مؤثرة في حدوث الفعل : محال . إنما قلنا : إنه ممتنع أن يحصل ذلك التأثير بتركه من ذلك المحل لأن ذلك المحل ليس له إلا كونه قابلاً للصفات أعني : أنه لا يمتنع حصول هذه الصفات فيه ، ولا يمتنع لا حصولها أيضاً فيه . فلو جعلنا المحل جزءاً من المؤثر، لكنا قد جعلنا القابل جزءاً من الموجد . وذلك محال . لأن هذه القابلية طبيعتها طبيعة الإمكان الخاص . والموجدية طبيعتها طبيعة الوجوب . والإمكان الخاص ينافي الوجوب . وكون المنافي جزء من المنافي الآخر محال في العقول . وإنما قلنا : إنه يمتنع أن يكون تأثير القدرة في حدوث ذلك الفعل ، لا شركة من ذلك المحل . وذلك لأن وجود الشيء جزء من كونه موجداً لغيره فلو كانت هذه القدرة غنية في مؤثريتها عن ذلك المحل ، لكانت غنية أيضاً في وجودها عن ذلك المحل. لأنه متى كان المركب غنياً عن شيء ، كان كل واحد من بسيطته أيضاً غنياً عنه . ثبت : أن هذه القدرة لو كانت في مؤثريتها في الفعل غنية عن ذلك المحل ، لكانت في وجودها أيضاً غنية عن ذلك المحل . وذلك محال . فثبت بما ذكرنا : أن القدرة لو كانت مؤثرة في وجود الفعل، لكان ذلك التأثير إما أن يكون بشركة من ذلك المحل ، أو لا بشركة من ذلك المخل ، وثبت كون كل واحد من هذين القسمين باطلاً . فثبت : أن القدرة غير مؤثرة في حدوث الفعل وهذا هو دليل الحكماء، على أن القوى الجسمانية غير مؤثرة في الوجود اصلا .
وبالله التوفيق
The ninth proof, and that is what is sought. If the servant's power were to effect the occurrence of the action, then its effect on the occurrence of that action would either be together with its substrate (the locus of that power) or not together with its substrate. Both divisions are false, so the statement that the servant's power is effective in causing the action is impossible. We say: It is impossible for that effect to occur together with its substrate because that substrate only has the property of being receptive to attributes, meaning: it is not impossible for these attributes to occur in it, nor is it impossible for them not to occur in it. If we make the substrate a part of the agent, then we have made the receiver a part of the creator, and that is impossible because the nature of this receptivity is the nature of particular possibility, while the nature of being a creator is the nature of necessity. Particular possibility contradicts necessity, and making a contradictory part of another contradictory is impossible in the intellect. And we say: It is impossible for the power's effect on the occurrence of that action to be without participation of that substrate, because the existence of a thing is part of its being a creator of another. If this power were independent in its effectiveness from that substrate, it would also be independent in its existence from that substrate, because whenever a composite is independent of something, each of its simples is also independent of it. It is established that if this power were independent in its effectiveness on the action from that substrate, it would also be independent in its existence from that substrate, and that is impossible. Thus it is established by what we have mentioned: if power were effective in the existence of the action, then that effect would either be with the participation of that substrate or without the participation of that substrate, and it is established that each of these two divisions is false. So it is established that power is not effective in causing the action, and this is the argument of the philosophers that corporeal powers are not effective in existence at all. And with God is the success.
Source pages p. 97

Commentary
This proof argues that the servant's power cannot be the agent causing the action. It divides the possibility into two: either the power's effect occurs together with its substrate (the body or locus of the power) or without it. Both are shown impossible. If the effect occurs with the substrate, then the substrate, which is merely receptive, becomes part of the agent, but receptivity (possibility) contradicts creative power (necessity). If the effect occurs without the substrate, then the power would be independent of the substrate in its effect, and thus also independent in its existence, which is false because the power exists only in the substrate. Therefore, the power cannot be effective. The proof concludes that corporeal powers are not effective in existence at all.

Key Terms
قدرة العبد
تأثير
محل
قابل
موجد
إمكان خاص
وجوب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that the servant's power is an independent agent that can cause actions on its own, without needing the substrate (body).
Correction: This proof shows that power cannot be independent because its existence depends on the substrate; if it were independent in effect, it would be independent in existence, which is false.

Checkpoint
Why is it impossible for the servant's power to be effective together with its substrate?
A) Because the substrate is not real
B) Because the substrate is receptive (possible) and the power is creative (necessary), and it is impossible for a contradictory to be part of another
C) Because the substrate cannot be known
D) Because the power does not exist

Key Takeaway
The servant's power cannot be the agent of the action because both possible scenarios (effect with or without its substrate) lead to logical contradictions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you feel that your own effort and power are the direct cause of your success, how does this proof challenge that sense of agency? In what specific areas of your life do you need to consciously acknowledge that your power is not truly effective without Allah's creation?
?
This argument shows that the power cannot be independent of its substrate. How does this make you reconsider your reliance on your own abilities? Are there actions you habitually attribute to yourself that you now realize are dependent on conditions you do not control?
?
If the servant's power is not a real agent, then what is your role in your actions? Let this proof humble you: write down one way you will actively remind yourself that Allah is the true creator of your deeds, starting today.

The Tenth Proof: A Short Argument by Contradiction
A brief proof by contradiction showing the impossibility of the servant creating his own action.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how a simple argument by contradiction (burhan al-luzum) refutes the claim that the servant creates his own actions.

Core Idea
If we created our actions, we would never experience anything we didn't will — but we do.

This proof is remarkably concise yet powerful. It uses the logical form of consequence (luzum) to show that the claim that servants create their actions leads to a false outcome. Since the outcome is false, the claim itself must be false. Let's walk through this tight argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Notice the concise and tight logical structure.
This proof is only two lines long, but every word carries weight.
2
Identify the two parts: ملزوم and لازم
The ملزوم (antecedent) is the claim that the servant creates his action. The لازم (consequent) is that he would never encounter anything except what he willed. See how they are connected by logical necessity.
Understanding this cause-effect relationship is key.
3
Understand why the لازم is false
Think of examples from your own experience. Do you always get what you will? Or do you sometimes experience things you do not will? The falsity of the consequent is obvious once you reflect on everyday life.
This refutes the claim that the servant is the creator of his actions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
البرهان العاشر
لو كان فعل العبد يحدث بإيجاد العبد ، لوجب أن لا يجد إلا ما أراده العبد . واللازم باطل ، فالملزوم مثله
The tenth proof: If the servant's action were to occur by the servant's creation, then it would be necessary that he does not encounter except what the servant willed. But the consequent is false, so the antecedent is likewise.
Source pages p. 98

Commentary
This is a very brief proof by contradiction (burhan al-luzum). It states that if the servant were the creator of his own actions, then he would never encounter (experience) any action except what he himself willed. That is, all his actions would conform to his will. But this is false because we often experience actions that we do not will or that happen against our will. Therefore, the premise (that the servant creates his own actions) is false.

Key Terms
إيجاد العبد
لازم
ملزوم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that if the servant creates his action, he must will it, so the consequence is true.
Correction: The proof shows that we sometimes experience actions we do not will, so creation cannot be by us.

Checkpoint
According to the tenth proof, what false consequence follows if the servant created his own action?
A) The action would be eternal
B) The servant would never encounter any action except what he willed
C) The action would be impossible
D) The servant would have no will

Key Takeaway
The servant does not create his own actions because we often experience actions that we do not will, contradicting the necessary consequence of such a claim.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you find yourself frustrated by something you said or did that you did not truly will, do you stop to realize that Allah alone created that action? How does this awareness change your response — from regret to turning back to Him?
?
If you truly internalized that every breath, every movement, every word you speak is created by Allah and not by your own power, how would it change your pride in your achievements and your despair over your failures? Commit today to affirming Allah as the sole Creator of all actions, even the ones you dislike.

Tenth Proof: Actions Not Created by the Servant
Author proves actions are not created by the servant through a conditional argument, with objection and partial answer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical proof that human actions cannot be independently created by the servant, and the response to the objection about ignorance leading to erroneous beliefs.

Core Idea
If the servant created his actions, he would always get what he intends; he doesn't, so he doesn't create them.

This lesson presents the tenth proof from al-Matalib al-Aliyah, which argues that human actions cannot be created by the servant because what actually occurs often contradicts the servant's intention. The proof is followed by an objection and its refutation, establishing that the initial ignorance must be created by God.

Guided Path
1
Read the proof structure
Read the Arabic passage carefully. Identify the conditional statement (if servant creates, then only intended actions occur), the explanation of the conditional, and the evidence that disproves it.
The proof is a modus tollens: if P then Q, not Q, therefore not P.
2
Examine the evidence
Look at the three examples: seeking true knowledge but getting ignorance, intending faith but getting disbelief, and the inability to perfectly replicate a line of writing. Notice how each shows the servant does not always get what he intends.
These are empirical observations.
3
Understand the objection
Read the objection: the servant's erroneous belief that his false conviction is knowledge explains why he chose it. See how the objector tries to preserve human choice.
The objection assumes ignorance can be chosen knowingly.
4
Analyze the first answer
The first answer argues that the initial ignorance must itself be explained; if it traces back to another ignorance, infinite regress follows; if chosen from the start, it's impossible. Therefore the first ignorance is created by God.
This establishes God's control over the origin of error.
5
Note the incomplete second answer
The second answer begins by stating that the servant has absolutely no choice, but it cuts off. This part is incomplete in the source.
This may be supplemented from other pages.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
البرهان العاشر
لو كان فعل العبد يحدث بإيجاد العبد ، لوجب أن لا يجد إلا ما أراده العبد . واللازم باطل ، فالملزوم مثله
بيان الشرطية : أن العبد لما كان قادراً على الأفعال المختلفة ، كان رجحان البعض على الباقي لا يكون إلا بقصده، فوجب أن لا يقع ، وان لا يحدث إلا ما قصده وأراده . وبيان أنه ليس كذلك : أن العبد يقصد تحصيل العلم الحق ، والاعتقاد الصواب ، فلا يحصل له ذلك ، بل يحصل له الجهل والباطل . ويقصد الإيمان ، فيحصل له الكفر . بل نقول : الإنسان إذا كتب سطراً ، فلو أتي بجميع حيل الدنيا ، حتى يكتب سطرا آخر ، يشابه الأول ، في الكيفيات المحسوسة ، لعجز عن ذلك . فثبت بما ذكرنا : أنه لو كان فعله بإيجاده ، لما وقع إلا ما قصد إيقاعه . وثبت : أنه ليس كذلك ، فوجب أن لا يكون فعله بإيجاده . وهذه الدلالة أخذتها من قول أمير المؤمنين علي بن أبي طالب رضي الله عنه : ( عرفت ربي بنقص العزائم ، وفسخ الهمم . )
فإن قيل : إنه إنما حصل له هذا الاعتقاد المخصوص ، لأنه ظن ان هذا الاعتقاد علم وحق وصواب. فلهذا السبب اختاره وحصله ورضي به . فالحاصل : أنه إن علم كونه جهلاً ، لما رضي به ، ولكنه لما اعتقد فيه أنه هو العلم . لا جرم رضي به واختاره .
والجواب : إن هذا باطل من وجهين :
الأول : إنه إنما قصد تحصيل هذا الاعتقاد . لأنه كان قد اعتقد أن هذا الاعتقاد : علم . فلولا تقدم ذلك الجهل ، وإلا لما رضي بهذا الجهل الثاني . فنقول : ذلك الجهل الأول ، كيف حصل ؟ فإن كان ذلك لتقدم جهل آخر ، لزم التسلسل . وإن كان ذلك لأنه اختار الجهل وارتضاه لنفسه ابتداء . فهذا محال . فلم يبق إلا أن يقال : الجهالات تترقى إلى جهل أول ، خلقه الله تعالى في العبد ابتداء . وذلك هو المطلوب
الوجه الثاني : أن من أنصف : علم أنه لا اختيار للعبد البتة في حصول
The tenth proof: If the servant's action occurred by the servant's creation, then nothing would occur except what the servant willed. The consequent is false, so the antecedent is likewise false. Explanation of the conditional: Since the servant is capable of various actions, the preference of one over the rest only occurs by his intention. So it is necessary that nothing occurs or happens except what he intended and willed. And the proof that it is not so: the servant intends to obtain true knowledge and correct belief, but he does not obtain that; rather, he obtains ignorance and falsehood. And he intends faith, but disbelief occurs to him. Rather, we say: a person writes a line; if he brings all the devices of the world to write another line similar to the first in sensible qualities, he would be incapable of that. So it is established by what we mentioned: if his action were by his creation, then nothing would occur except what he intended to bring about. And it is established that it is not so. So it is necessary that his action is not by his creation. And this indication I took from the saying of the Commander of the Faithful 'Ali ibn Abi Talib, may God be pleased with him: "I recognized my Lord by the failure of determinations and the dissolution of resolves." If it is said: He only obtained this particular belief because he thought that this belief is knowledge, truth, and correctness. For this reason he chose it, obtained it, and was pleased with it. So the result: if he knew it to be ignorance, he would not be pleased with it, but because he believed it to be knowledge, he was inevitably pleased with it and chose it. The answer: This is false in two respects: First: He only intended to obtain this belief because he had believed that this belief is knowledge. Were it not for the precedence of that ignorance, he would not have been pleased with this second ignorance. We say: how did that first ignorance occur? If it was due to the precedence of another ignorance, an infinite regress follows. And if it was because he chose ignorance and approved of it for himself from the beginning, that is impossible. So nothing remains except to say that ignorances trace back to a first ignorance which God, exalted, created in the servant from the beginning. That is the desired conclusion. Second: Whoever is fair knows that the servant has absolutely no choice in the occurrence of...
Source pages p. 98

Commentary
This passage presents the tenth proof (البرهان العاشر) that human actions are not created by the servant. It begins with a conditional statement: if the servant created his own action, then only what he wills would happen; but the consequent is false, so the antecedent is false. The conditional is explained: since the servant has power over multiple actions, preference for one over another must be by his intention (قصد); so only intended actions would occur. Reality contradicts this: the servant intends true knowledge but gets ignorance, intends faith but gets disbelief. Even in writing, one cannot perfectly replicate a line. These examples show that actions are not under the servant's full control, so they cannot be created by him. The author attributes this argument to 'Ali (رضي الله عنه). An objection then arises: the servant's mistaken belief that his erroneous conviction is knowledge explains why he chose it. The answer refutes this in two ways. The first way argues that the initial ignorance (that led to the choice) must itself be explained: if it traces back to a prior ignorance, infinite regress results; if it was chosen freely, that is impossible. Therefore, the first ignorance must be created by God. The second way (incomplete in the text) begins by stating that the truly fair-minded person knows the servant has absolutely no choice in what occurs.

Key Terms
إيجاد
قصد
شرطية
جهل
تسلسل
اختيار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think this argument denies human action altogether, claiming humans have no role.
Correction: The argument does not deny that humans perform actions; it denies that actions are independently created by human power. Actions are created by God while humans acquire them (kasb), consistent with Ash'ari theology.

Checkpoint
What is the basis of the conditional (شرطية) in the tenth proof?
A) The servant is capable of various actions, so preference requires intention, meaning only intended actions would occur.
B) The servant always gets exactly what he intends.
C) The servant cannot write two identical lines because of lack of power.
D) The servant's power is independent of God's creation.

Key Takeaway
The tenth proof shows that since humans do not always get what they intend, their actions cannot be created by them; instead, God is the sole creator, and the initial ignorance that leads to error is also created by God.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever desperately intended to do good—to pray sincerely, to speak kindly, to understand a truth—but instead found yourself failing or falling into the opposite? How does this lesson confront your assumption that you are the master of your own actions?
?
Think of a time you were absolutely certain you were right, only to later discover you were mistaken. Where did that first ignorance come from? Can you trace it back to a cause within you, or must you admit that God created it in you? How does that realization humble your pride?
?
If even your failures and errors are ultimately created by God, then what is left for you to claim as your own? What does this lesson demand of you in terms of reliance on God and gratitude for every good that occurs through you?

The Proof from Intention-Outcome Discrepancy
Sanusi presents a conditional proof and empirical counter-examples to deny human origination of actions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the main Ash'ari proof that human actions are not originated by human will, based on the observed discrepancy between intentions and outcomes.

Core Idea
If we truly originated our actions, intentions would always match results — but they don't.

This lesson presents the foundational argument from Ash'ari theology: if human actions were originated by the human agent, then every action would exactly conform to intention. Yet we observe people intending knowledge but obtaining ignorance, intending faith but obtaining disbelief. This mismatch proves that actions are not under our ultimate origination. The argument is attributed to Imam Ali.

Guided Path
1
Understand the conditional premise
Read the first sentence carefully. It states that if actions were from the servant, only intended actions would occur.
2
Examine the counter-evidence
Notice the examples: intending knowledge but getting ignorance, intending faith but getting disbelief. These show a clear mismatch.
3
Reflect on the handwriting example
Consider the strong example: a person cannot replicate a line of handwriting exactly, even with all the world's devices. This shows lack of control over actions.
4
Grasp the conclusion
Since intentions and outcomes do not always align, actions cannot be originated solely by the servant.
5
Note the source
Recognise that this argument is attributed to Imam Ali, showing its traditional roots in early Islamic thought.

بيان الشرطية : أن العبد لما كان قادراً على الأفعال المختلفة ، كان رجحان البعض على الباقي لا يكون إلا بقصده، فوجب أن لا يقع ، وان لا يحدث إلا ما قصده وأراده . وبيان أنه ليس كذلك : أن العبد يقصد تحصيل العلم الحق ، والاعتقاد الصواب ، فلا يحصل له ذلك ، بل يحصل له الجهل والباطل . ويقصد الإيمان ، فيحصل له الكفر . بل نقول : الإنسان إذا كتب سطراً ، فلو أتي بجميع حيل الدنيا ، حتى يكتب سطرا آخر ، يشابه الأول ، في الكيفيات المحسوسة ، لعجز عن ذلك . فثبت بما ذكرنا : أنه لو كان فعله بإيجاده ، لما وقع إلا ما قصد إيقاعه . وثبت : أنه ليس كذلك ، فوجب أن لا يكون فعله بإيجاده . وهذه الدلالة أخذتها من قول أمير المؤمنين علي بن أبي طالب رضي الله عنه : ( عرفت ربي بنقص العزائم ، وفسخ الهمم ) .
فإن قيل : إنه إنما حصل له هذا الاعتقاد المخصوص ، لأنه ظن ان هذا الاعتقاد علم وحق وصواب. فلهذا السبب اختاره وحصله ورضي به . فالحاصل : أنه إن علم كونه جهلاً ، لما رضي به ، ولكنه لما اعتقد فيه أنه هو العلم . لا جرم رضي به واختاره .
والجواب : إن هذا باطل من وجهين :
الأول : إنه إنما قصد تحصيل هذا الاعتقاد . لأنه كان قد اعتقد أن هذا الاعتقاد : علم . فلولا تقدم ذلك الجهل ، وإلا لما رضي بهذا الجهل الثاني . فنقول : ذلك الجهل الأول ، كيف حصل ؟ فإن كان [ ذلك ] لتقدم جهل آخر ، لزم التسلسل . وإن [ كان ] ذلك لأنه اختار الجهل وارتضاه لنفسه ابتداء . فهذا محال . فلم يبق إلا أن يقال : الجهالات تترقى إلى جهل أول ، خلقه الله تعالى في العبد ابتداء . وذلك هو المطلوب
الوجه الثاني : أن من أنصف : علم أنه لا اختيار للعبد البتة في حصول الجهل [ له ] وذلك لأنه ما لم يترتب في ذهنه مقدمات موجبة لهذا الجهل ، لم يحصل في قلبه هذا الجهل . ثم الكلام في تلك المقدمات كما في هذه النتيجة . فتلك الجهالات لا بد أن تترقى إلى جهل أول وقع في قلبه . لا منه ، ولا بسعيه ولا بطلب الحصول ذلك الجهل الأول في قلبه ، وتأدى ذلك الجهل إلى سائر الجهالات اللازمة منه : ليس أيضاً بسعيه ولا باختياره . فثبت : أن جميع الجهالات إنما تحصل في العبد على سبيل الاضطرار، ولا على سبيل الاختيار .
Statement of the conditional: that when the servant is capable of various actions, the preference of some over the rest is only by his intention, so it is necessary that nothing occurs or happens except what he intended and willed. And the statement that it is not so: that the servant intends to obtain true knowledge and correct belief, but he does not obtain that; rather he obtains ignorance and falsehood. And he intends faith, but he obtains disbelief. Rather we say: if a person writes a line, even if he brings all the contrivances of the world in order to write another line similar to the first in sensible qualities, he would be incapable of that. So it is established by what we have mentioned: that if his action were by his own origination, nothing would occur except what he intended to bring about. And it is established that it is not so, so it is necessary that his action is not by his own origination. And this indication I took from the saying of the Commander of the Faithful, Ali ibn Abi Talib, may Allah be pleased with him: 'I knew my Lord by the deficiency of resolves and the annulment of determinations.' So if it is said: He only obtained this particular belief because he thought that this belief is knowledge, truth, and correctness. For this reason he chose it, obtained it, and was pleased with it. In sum: if he knew it to be ignorance, he would not have been pleased with it, but because he believed it to be knowledge, he was necessarily pleased with it and chose it. The answer: This is false in two respects: First: He only intended to obtain this belief because he had believed that this belief is knowledge. So were it not for that prior ignorance, he would not have been pleased with this second ignorance. So we say: That first ignorance, how did it occur? If it was due to a preceding another ignorance, then an infinite regress would follow. And if it occurred because he chose ignorance and approved it for himself initially, this is impossible. So nothing remains but to say: ignorances ascend to a first ignorance that Allah Almighty created in the servant initially. And that is the desired conclusion. The second aspect: Whoever is fair knows that the servant has absolutely no choice in the occurrence of ignorance for him. This is because as long as premises necessitating this ignorance are not arranged in his mind, this ignorance does not occur in his heart. Then the discussion about those premises is like the discussion about this conclusion. So those ignorances must ascend to a first ignorance that occurred in his heart, neither from him nor by his effort nor by his seeking the occurrence of that first ignorance in his heart, and that ignorance led to the rest of the ignorances consequent upon it: also not by his effort or his choice. So it is established that all ignorances only occur in the servant by way of compulsion, not by way of choice.
Source pages p. 99

Commentary
This passage presents a proof from Ash'ari theology that human actions are not originated by human power or will, but are created by God. It begins with a conditional statement: since the human servant is capable of various actions, the preference of one action over another must be by his intention; therefore, if the action were truly originated by him, only what he intends would occur. The author then shows the contrary: the servant intends to obtain true knowledge and correct belief, but often obtains ignorance and falsehood. He intends faith but obtains disbelief. A stronger example: a person cannot reproduce an identical handwritten line even with all devices. Thus, if actions were originated by the servant, they would always match intention, but they don't; therefore actions are not originated by him. This argument is attributed to Imam Ali. The author then presents an objection: perhaps the servant obtains ignorance because he mistakenly believes it is knowledge, so he chooses it willingly. The answer has two parts. First, this assumes a prior belief (that the ignorance is knowledge), which itself is an ignorance; that prior ignorance must be explained. If it stems from another ignorance, there is infinite regress; if it is chosen initially, that is impossible. Thus, all ignorances trace back to a first ignorance created by God. Second, a fair-minded person sees that the servant has no choice at all in the occurrence of ignorance, because ignorance only follows from premises in the mind, and the premises themselves are not under his control. This leads to an infinite regress of ignorances unless there is a first ignorance imposed by God. The conclusion: all ignorances occur in the servant by compulsion, not by choice. This reinforces the Ash'ari doctrine that human actions and their attendant states are ultimately created by God.

Key Terms
قصد
إيجاد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Humans fully originate their own actions through their will and power.
Correction: The passage shows that if humans originated their actions, intentions would always match outcomes. Since they do not, actions cannot be fully originated by humans; they are created by God.

Checkpoint
What does the handwriting example in the passage illustrate?
A) The power of human will to produce identical results
B) That humans cannot reproduce an identical line, proving actions are not from their origination
C) That humans can control every detail of their actions
D) That God does not create actions

Key Takeaway
The discrepancy between intention and outcome proves that human actions are not originated by the human agent alone.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a time when you sincerely intended to do something good—like speak the truth or help someone—but ended up failing or doing the opposite. What does that moment reveal about who truly controls your actions? Are you willing to admit that your own power is limited, and that God's will prevails? Will you stop pretending you are the master of your actions and instead turn to God in humility?
?
The inability to replicate a single line of handwriting shows how little control you have over even the simplest physical act. Does your daily life reflect a reliance on your own ability or a dependence on God's creation of your actions? What would change if you truly lived as if every move, every word, every thought is given by God?
?
Imam Ali said he recognized Allah through the deficiency of resolves. When was the last time your own plans were overturned? Did you respond with frustration or with recognition of Allah's sovereignty? How can you train yourself to see every broken intention as a sign of Allah's control, not as a personal failure?

Divine Decree

Responding to the Objection: Infinite Regress and Divine Origination
Sanusi refutes an objection using two arguments about infinite regress and compulsion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand two arguments that refute the objection that ignorance is chosen because it is mistaken for knowledge, leading to the conclusion that all ignorance traces back to a first ignorance created by God.

Core Idea
Every ignorance leads back to a first ignorance that God creates in the heart.

The previous lesson proved that actions are not from human origination. But an objector might say that people choose ignorance because they mistakenly think it is knowledge. This lesson provides a two-part answer: first, that such a belief only pushes the problem back to an earlier ignorance, leading to an infinite regress unless there is a first ignorance from God. Second, that the premises leading to ignorance are not under our control, so ignorance occurs by compulsion.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
Read the objection: the servant obtains ignorance because he mistakenly believes it is knowledge and thus chooses it willingly.
2
Follow the first answer
The first answer argues that this assumed prior belief (that ignorance is knowledge) is itself an ignorance, leading to an infinite regress unless there is a first ignorance created by God.
3
Grasp the conclusion of the first argument
The only way to stop the regress is to posit a first ignorance created by Allah in the servant initially.
4
Examine the second answer
The second answer shows that the premises leading to ignorance are not under the servant's control, so ignorance occurs by compulsion, not choice.
5
Recognize the final conclusion
All ignorances in the servant occur by way of compulsion, not by choice, reinforcing the Ash'ari doctrine.

The Impossibility of Infinite Regress and Circularity in Knowledge
States the premise that acquired knowledge must end in self-evident truths.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why acquired knowledge must terminate in self-evident truths to avoid infinite regress or circularity.

Core Idea
All acquired knowledge rests on self-evident foundations; otherwise, reasoning collapses.

This passage presents the first proof that knowledge cannot be purely acquired from prior knowledge indefinitely. Without a stopping point at self-evident truths, we face an infinite chain or a circle, both of which are logically impossible. Let us examine how the author establishes this foundational principle.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to grasp the initial claim: that every knowledge cannot be acquired from another prior knowledge.
Pay attention to the two terms 'tasalsul' and 'dawr'.
2
Identify the two problems
The text says that if every knowledge were acquired from a prior one, it would lead to either infinite regress (tasalsul) or circularity (dawr). Understand why both are unacceptable in logical reasoning.
Infinite regress never reaches a foundation; circularity assumes what is to be proven.
3
Recognize the solution
The author concludes that acquired knowledges must terminate in knowledges present in the intellect not by acquisition—the self-evident truths (badīhiyyāt).
These are the foundational building blocks of all knowledge.
4
Reflect on the context
Consider that this argument is part of a larger discussion about faith and disbelief being created by God. The epistemological grounding supports a deterministic view of knowledge acquisition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
العوائل الحالة على أن حصول الإيمان والكفر في قليب السباء لا يمكن أن يكون إلا بتخيل الله تعالى البرهان الأول إنه لا يمكن أن يكون كل علم مكتسباً من علم آخر قبله وإلا لزم [ إما (۱) ] التسلسل ، وإما الدور . بل لا بد وأن تنتهي هذه المكتسبات إلى علوم حاصلة في العقل ، لا على سبيل الاكتساب ، وهي البديهيات إذا عرفت هذا فنقول : كل ما لا بد منه في كون تلك البديهيات مستلزمة لهذه الكسبيات . إما أن يكون حاصلاً ، وإما أن لا يكون . فإن كان الأول وجب كون هذه المكتسبات حاصلة عند حصول تلك البديهيات . لأن المؤثر ، إذا كان مستجمعاً لجميع الجهات المعتبرة في المؤثرية ، فإنه يمتنع تخلف الأثر ( عنه (۲) ) فإذا كانت تلك البديهيات غير مقدورة البتة ، وكان استلزامها لهذه النظريات استلزاماً ضرورياً ، غير داخل تحت الاختيار . وجب القطع بأن هذه النتائج غير داخلة تحت القدرة والاختيار . وأما إن قلنا : إن تلك البديهيات غير مستجمعة لجميع الجهات المعتبرة في استلزام هذه النظريات ، فحينئذ لا بد من ضم أمور أخرى إليها . وتلك الأمور. إما أن تكون من العلوم البديهية ، أو
The [؟] condition that the occurrence of faith and disbelief in the heart of the child cannot be except through the creation of God, the Most High. The first proof: It is impossible for every knowledge to be acquired from another knowledge before it, otherwise either infinite regress or circularity would be necessary. Rather, these acquired [knowledges] must terminate in knowledges present in the intellect, not by way of acquisition, and they are the self-evident truths (al-badīhiyyāt). If you understand this, then we say: Everything that is necessary for those self-evident truths to entail these acquired [knowledges]—either it is present, or it is not. If it is the first [i.e., present], then it is necessary that these acquired [knowledges] occur when those self-evident truths occur, because the effective cause (al-muʼaththir), when it has gathered all the aspects considered in effectiveness, it is impossible for the effect to be delayed from it. So when those self-evident truths are absolutely not within power, and their entailment of these theoretical [truths] is a necessary entailment, not subject to choice, then it must be decisively concluded that these results are not within power and choice. And if we say that those self-evident truths are not gathered with all the aspects considered in entailing these theoretical [truths], then at that time there is no avoiding the addition of other things to them. And those things are either from the self-evident sciences, or
Source pages p. 102

Commentary
This passage presents a proof (burhān) concerning the foundation of knowledge, specifically arguing that acquired knowledge must rest on self-evident truths (al-badīhiyyāt). The context is a discussion about faith and disbelief in the child's heart being solely through God's creation, but the proof itself is a general epistemological argument.

First, the author states the impossibility that every science (ʿilm) is acquired from another science preceding it, because that would lead to either infinite regress (tasalsul) or circularity (dawr). Therefore, acquired knowledges must terminate in knowledges that are present in the intellect not by acquisition—these are the self-evident truths (al-badīhiyyāt).

Having established that, the author then examines what is necessary for those self-evident truths to entail the acquired (or theoretical) truths. He presents a dichotomy: either that necessary condition is present or it is not.

If it is present, then when the self-evident truths are present, the acquired truths must also be present. This is because the effective cause (al-muʼaththir), when it possesses all the aspects required for effectiveness, necessarily produces its effect without delay. Since the self-evident truths are absolutely beyond human power (ghayr maqdūrah) and their entailment of the theoretical truths is necessary and not subject to choice, it follows that these results (the acquired truths) are also not within human power and choice.

Alternatively, if the self-evident truths are not fully sufficient (i.e., they lack some aspect needed to entail the theoretical truths), then other things must be added. These additions could themselves be self-evident (the text ends here, cutting off before completing the dichotomy).

Thus, the proof aims to show that ultimately all knowledge, whether faith or other acquired beliefs, is grounded in necessary, non-optional foundations, which has implications for divine predestination (qadar) and human responsibility.

Key Terms
البديهيات
التسلسل
الدور
المكتسبات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What two logical problems would arise if every science were acquired from a prior one?
A) Infinite regress and circularity
B) Self-evident truths and axioms
C) Contradiction and doubt
D) Tautology and paradox

Key Takeaway
Acquired knowledge must ultimately rest on self-evident truths to avoid logical absurdities.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of one deeply held belief you have about God or the afterlife. Can you trace its foundation to a self-evident truth in your intellect, or does it rely on another acquired belief? What happens to your certainty if that chain is infinite?
?
When you doubt a theological teaching, do you immediately assume your intellect is flawed, or do you consider that the teaching might not rest on self-evident principles? How does this lesson hold you accountable to examine the basis of both your faith and your doubts?
?
If you were to explain to someone why they cannot rely solely on acquired proofs without end, what self-evident truth would you point to as the ultimate foundation? Are you yourself living as if that foundation is secure, or do you act on assumptions without grounding them?

The Necessity of Self-Evident Truths and Their Implications for Human Power
Analyzes the sufficiency of self-evident truths and its implications.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the conditional argument about whether self-evident truths are sufficient to entail acquired knowledge, and the consequences for human free will.

Core Idea
If self-evident truths are sufficient, their effects are necessary and beyond human choice.

Having established that knowledge terminates in self-evident truths, the author now examines whether these truths alone are enough to produce acquired knowledge. The analysis leads to a profound conclusion: if they are sufficient, then what follows from them is necessary and not subject to human control, which has direct bearing on divine predestination.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the two cases
The passage presents a dichotomy: either the necessary condition for the entailment is present (i.e., the self-evident truths are fully sufficient) or it is not.
Note the phrase 'إما أن يكون حاصلاً، وإما أن لا يكون'.
2
Understand the logic of the effective cause
The author argues that if the effective cause (al-muʼaththir) has all aspects necessary for effectiveness, the effect cannot be delayed. This is a principle of necessary causation.
This principle is used to assert that if self-evident truths are fully sufficient, the acquired knowledge must follow immediately.
3
Apply to self-evident truths and human power
Since the self-evident truths are not within our power (ghayr maqdūrah) and their entailment is necessary, the resulting knowledge is also beyond power and choice. Consider the implication for faith and disbelief.
This ties into the broader debate on qadar (divine decree).
4
Consider the alternative case
If the self-evident truths are not fully sufficient, additional factors are needed, which could themselves be self-evident. The passage cuts off, but the regress continues.
This alternative does not undermine the main conclusion; it merely postpones the necessity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
العوائل الحالة على أن حصول الإيمان والكفر في قليب السباء لا يمكن أن يكون إلا بتخيل الله تعالى البرهان الأول إنه لا يمكن أن يكون كل علم مكتسباً من علم آخر قبله وإلا لزم [ إما (۱) ] التسلسل ، وإما الدور . بل لا بد وأن تنتهي هذه المكتسبات إلى علوم حاصلة في العقل ، لا على سبيل الاكتساب ، وهي البديهيات إذا عرفت هذا فنقول : كل ما لا بد منه في كون تلك البديهيات مستلزمة لهذه الكسبيات . إما أن يكون حاصلاً ، وإما أن لا يكون . فإن كان الأول وجب كون هذه المكتسبات حاصلة عند حصول تلك البديهيات . لأن المؤثر ، إذا كان مستجمعاً لجميع الجهات المعتبرة في المؤثرية ، فإنه يمتنع تخلف الأثر ( عنه (۲) ) فإذا كانت تلك البديهيات غير مقدورة البتة ، وكان استلزامها لهذه النظريات استلزاماً ضرورياً ، غير داخل تحت الاختيار . وجب القطع بأن هذه النتائج غير داخلة تحت القدرة والاختيار . وأما إن قلنا : إن تلك البديهيات غير مستجمعة لجميع الجهات المعتبرة في استلزام هذه النظريات ، فحينئذ لا بد من ضم أمور أخرى إليها . وتلك الأمور. إما أن تكون من العلوم البديهية ، أو
The [؟] condition that the occurrence of faith and disbelief in the heart of the child cannot be except through the creation of God, the Most High. The first proof: It is impossible for every knowledge to be acquired from another knowledge before it, otherwise either infinite regress or circularity would be necessary. Rather, these acquired [knowledges] must terminate in knowledges present in the intellect, not by way of acquisition, and they are the self-evident truths (al-badīhiyyāt). If you understand this, then we say: Everything that is necessary for those self-evident truths to entail these acquired [knowledges]—either it is present, or it is not. If it is the first [i.e., present], then it is necessary that these acquired [knowledges] occur when those self-evident truths occur, because the effective cause (al-muʼaththir), when it has gathered all the aspects considered in effectiveness, it is impossible for the effect to be delayed from it. So when those self-evident truths are absolutely not within power, and their entailment of these theoretical [truths] is a necessary entailment, not subject to choice, then it must be decisively concluded that these results are not within power and choice. And if we say that those self-evident truths are not gathered with all the aspects considered in entailing these theoretical [truths], then at that time there is no avoiding the addition of other things to them. And those things are either from the self-evident sciences, or
Source pages p. 102

Commentary
This passage presents a proof (burhān) concerning the foundation of knowledge, specifically arguing that acquired knowledge must rest on self-evident truths (al-badīhiyyāt). The context is a discussion about faith and disbelief in the child's heart being solely through God's creation, but the proof itself is a general epistemological argument.

First, the author states the impossibility that every science (ʿilm) is acquired from another science preceding it, because that would lead to either infinite regress (tasalsul) or circularity (dawr). Therefore, acquired knowledges must terminate in knowledges that are present in the intellect not by acquisition—these are the self-evident truths (al-badīhiyyāt).

Having established that, the author then examines what is necessary for those self-evident truths to entail the acquired (or theoretical) truths. He presents a dichotomy: either that necessary condition is present or it is not.

If it is present, then when the self-evident truths are present, the acquired truths must also be present. This is because the effective cause (al-muʼaththir), when it possesses all the aspects required for effectiveness, necessarily produces its effect without delay. Since the self-evident truths are absolutely beyond human power (ghayr maqdūrah) and their entailment of the theoretical truths is necessary and not subject to choice, it follows that these results (the acquired truths) are also not within human power and choice.

Alternatively, if the self-evident truths are not fully sufficient (i.e., they lack some aspect needed to entail the theoretical truths), then other things must be added. These additions could themselves be self-evident (the text ends here, cutting off before completing the dichotomy).

Thus, the proof aims to show that ultimately all knowledge, whether faith or other acquired beliefs, is grounded in necessary, non-optional foundations, which has implications for divine predestination (qadar) and human responsibility.

Misconception: Human choice can override necessary entailments from foundational truths.
Correction: According to the argument, if self-evident truths are fully sufficient, their consequences are necessary and not subject to human power or choice.

B) The acquired knowledge is not within power

D) Self-evident truths are not necessary

Key Takeaway

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Or does it increase your accountability to seek the truth? How will you ensure that your convictions are based on sound reasoning rather than mere habit?
?
If some of your knowledge is beyond your power, then your ignorance in some matters may also be beyond your control. Does that thought humble you or frighten you? Will you now strive to identify which of your beliefs are truly self-evident and which require deeper investigation, even if that effort is uncomfortable?
?
The argument suggests that a complete cause cannot be delayed. Are there areas in your life where you are waiting for a 'sign' or a 'feeling' before acting, even though the cause for action is already complete? The Foundation: Self-Evident Truths Are Not Acquired
Sanusi presents the first proof: knowledge cannot all be acquired.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why not all knowledge can be acquired and why knowledge must terminate in self-evident truths that are not within human power.

Core Idea
Knowledge must stop somewhere: self-evident truths are the bedrock.

This lesson introduces the first part of Sanusi's proof. He begins by asserting that faith and disbelief are only by God's creation, then argues that if all knowledge were acquired from prior knowledge, it would lead to an infinite regress or circularity. Therefore, there must be basic truths that are not acquired but present in the intellect from the start.

Guided Path
1
Read the opening claim
Notice the claim that faith and disbelief can only be by God's creation. This sets the topic.
The phrase 'قلب الصبي' (heart of the child) is likely meant.
2
Understand the regress argument
Follow the logic: if every knowledge is acquired from a prior knowledge, you never reach a starting point. This is either an infinite chain (tasalsul) or a circle (dawr), both impossible.
3
Identify the conclusion about self-evident truths
The only solution is that there are knowledges present in the intellect without acquisition – these are the badihat (self-evident truths).
These are the foundation for all acquired knowledge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
على أن حصول الإيمان والكفر في [قليب السباء] لا يمكن أن يكون إلا [بتخيل] الله تعالى

البرهان الأول

إنه لا يمكن أن يكون كل علم مكتسباً من علم آخر قبله وإلا لزم إما التسلسل، وإما الدور. بل لا بد وأن تنتهي هذه المكتسبات إلى علوم حاصلة في العقل، لا على سبيل الاكتساب، وهي البديهيات.

إذا عرفت هذا فنقول: كل ما لا بد منه في كون تلك البديهيات مستلزمة لهذه الكسبيات، إما أن يكون حاصلاً، وإما أن لا يكون. فإن كان الأول وجب كون هذه المكتسبات حاصلة عند حصول تلك البديهيات، لأن المؤثر، إذا كان مستجمعاً لجميع الجهات المعتبرة في المؤثرية، فإنه يمتنع تخلف الأثر عنه. فإذا كانت تلك البديهيات غير مقدورة البتة، وكان استلزامها لهذه النظريات استلزاماً ضرورياً، غير داخل تحت الاختيار، وجب القطع بأن هذه النتائج غير داخلة تحت القدرة والاختيار. وأما إن قلنا: إن تلك البديهيات غير مستجمعة لجميع الجهات المعتبرة في استلزام هذه النظريات، فحينئذ لا بد من ضم أمور أخرى إليها. وتلك الأمور إما أن تكون من العلوم البديهية، أو من العلوم الكسبية. والأول باطل لأنا فرضنا حصول جميع العلوم البديهية. والثاني أيضاً باطل، لأن كلامنا في كيفية استلزام البديهيات لأول المكتسبات. وعلى هذا التقدير، يلزم أن يحصل قبل أول المكتسبات: مكتسب آخر. وذلك محال، لأن الذي يكون موصوفاً بأنه أول المكتسبات، يمتنع أن يحصل قبله مكتسب آخر. فثبت بما ذكرنا: أن البديهيات غير داخلة تحت القدرة. وثبت: أن استلزامها للمكتسب الأول غير داخل تحت الوسع. واستلزام المكتسب الأول للمكتسب الثاني، غير داخل أيضاً تحت القدرة. وهلم جرا في جميع المراتب، بالغة ما بلغت. فثبت: أن شيئاً من العلوم والمعارف غير واقع بقدرة العبد واختياره.

وهو المطلوب
That obtaining faith and disbelief in [the heart of the child] cannot be except by [the creation] of Allah Almighty.

The First Proof:

It is impossible that every knowledge is acquired from another knowledge before it, otherwise it would necessitate either infinite regress or circularity. Rather, these acquired [knowledges] must terminate at knowledges present in the intellect, not by acquisition, and they are the self-evident truths.

If you know this, we say: Everything necessary for those self-evident truths to entail these acquired [knowledges] is either present or not. If it is the first, then these acquired [knowledges] must be present when those self-evident truths are present, because if the cause possesses all the aspects considered in causality, it is impossible for the effect to lag behind it. So if those self-evident truths are absolutely not within power, and their entailment of these theoretical [knowledges] is a necessary entailment, not under choice, then it must be judged that these results are not under power and choice. And if we say that those self-evident truths do not possess all the aspects considered in entailing these theoretical [knowledges], then it is necessary to add other things to them. Those things are either from self-evident knowledges or from acquired knowledges. The first is false because we assumed the presence of all self-evident knowledges. The second is also false, because our discussion concerns how self-evident truths entail the first acquired [knowledge]. On this assumption, it would necessitate that before the first acquired [knowledge] another acquired [knowledge] occurs, which is impossible, because that which is described as the first acquired [knowledge] cannot have another acquired [knowledge] before it. So it is established by what we mentioned: that self-evident truths are not under power. And it is established: that their entailment of the first acquired [knowledge] is not within capacity. And the entailment of the first acquired [knowledge] to the second acquired [knowledge] is also not under power, and so on through all levels, as far as they go. So it is established: that nothing of knowledges and cognitions occurs by the power and choice of the servant.

And that is what was sought.
Source pages p. 102

Commentary
This passage presents the first proof that knowledge and faith are not within human power but are created by God. The author begins with a claim that faith and disbelief in the heart can only be by God's creation. Then he argues that if all knowledge were acquired from prior knowledge, it would lead to an infinite regress or circularity. Therefore, there must be basic self-evident knowledges that are innate. Next, he considers the entailment from these basic knowledges to acquired knowledges. If the basic knowledges are sufficient for the entailment, then the acquired knowledges must necessarily follow, since a complete cause cannot be without its effect. If the basic knowledges are insufficient, then additional factors are needed. Those factors cannot be self-evident (since all self-evident are already assumed) nor acquired (because that would require a prior acquired before the first acquired, which is impossible). Thus, the entailment itself is necessary and not under human choice. Since the basic knowledges are not chosen, and the entailment is necessary, all resulting knowledge is outside human power. The proof concludes that none of the sciences and cognitions occur by the servant's power and choice.

C) It would make knowledge impossible.
D) It would require self-evident truths.

Key Takeaway
Self-evident truths are the necessary foundation for all acquired knowledge and are not chosen by humans.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
What is one self-evident truth about Allah that you know without any proof? If you cannot think of one, does that mean you have never truly internalized it? Write it down and commit to affirming it daily until it becomes as certain as your own existence.
?
When you doubt a core belief, do you retreat into a chain of arguments or do you return to what you know innately? Identify one doubt you have and trace it back to the self-evident foundation it rests on—or admit you have no foundation and need to seek one.
?
If all your knowledge about faith were stripped away, what would remain in your heart as undeniable? Ponder that bedrock, and then ask yourself: have I been building my life on that, or on opinions I acquired without checking their roots?

Divine Decree

8 min
The Inevitable Chain: Why Knowledge Must Be Divine
Continuation of the proof: analyzing entailment and concluding divine creation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the entailment from self-evident truths to acquired knowledge must be necessary and not under human control, leading to the conclusion that all knowledge is created by God.

Core Idea
If the cause is complete, the effect must follow – and it's all outside our power.

Continuing the proof, Sanusi now examines the relationship between self-evident truths and the knowledge acquired from them. He argues that either the self-evident truths are sufficient to entail the acquired knowledge or not. If sufficient, the acquired knowledge must necessarily follow, and since the self-evident truths are not chosen, the resulting knowledge is also not chosen. If insufficient, adding more factors leads to an impossible regress. Therefore, all knowledge is beyond human power.

Guided Path
1
Understand the two scenarios
The author presents a conditional: either the self-evident truths contain all necessary conditions to produce acquired knowledge, or they lack some conditions.
2
Analyze the first scenario: sufficient cause
If they are sufficient, then the effect must be present immediately. Since the cause (self-evident truths) is not within human power, the effect (acquired knowledge) also is not.
This relies on the rule: when the complete cause exists, the effect cannot lag.
3
Analyze the second scenario: insufficient cause
If they are insufficient, additional things are needed. Those cannot be other self-evident truths (all are already assumed) nor acquired knowledges (that would require a prior acquired knowledge before the first acquired, which is impossible).
This leads to a contradiction, proving the first scenario is necessary.
4
Conclude the proof
Thus the entailment from self-evident truths to the first acquired knowledge is necessary and not under choice. The same applies to all subsequent acquired knowledges, so nothing of knowledge is within human power.
This establishes that all knowledge is created by God.

Source Text

Divine Decree
beginner
4 min
Knowledge is Beyond Human Power
The author argues that belief is necessary upon presence of concepts, so knowledge is not within human power.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the argument that genuine knowledge, whether concepts or attestations, is not within human power but is created by Allah.

Core Idea
Genuine knowledge cannot be acquired; it is either necessary or it is not knowledge.

This passage presents a concise argument that the very nature of knowledge precludes it from being under human control. The author analyzes the relationship between concepts and attestations to show that neither can be subject to human acquisition. Let's examine the reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to grasp the flow.
Notice the two conditional scenarios.
2
Identify the two cases
The author contrasts necessary attestation with non-necessary attestation. Mark the outcomes for each.
The first case leads to no power, the second leads to blind belief.
3
Trace the conclusion
See how the author concludes that both concepts and attestations are outside human power.
The final sentence attributes all knowledge to Allah's creation.
4
Reflect on the implication
Consider how this argument refutes the idea that humans acquire knowledge by their own power.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
التصديق عند حضور تلك التصورات لازماً أو ضرورياً ، لم يكن للعبد قدرة عليه ، ولا اختيار له فيه . لأن تلك التصورات لا قدرة للعبد عليها البتة . وعند حضورها تكون مستلزمة لذلك التصديق استلزاماً لا قدرة للعبد عليه فعلى هذا التقدير ، امتنع أن يكون ذلك التصديق واقعاً بكسب العبد و باختياره. وأما إن كان حصول التصديق عند حصول (۱) تلك التصورات غير ضروري ولا لازم ، فحينئذ لم يكن ذلك التصديق علماً ولا يقيناً ، بل هو اعتقاد تقليدي ، أتى به الإنسان من غير موجب . وهو أيضاً محال . ومتى حاول الإنسان تشكيك نفسه فيه ، أمكن ذلك وقبل هذا لا يكون علماً ولا يقيناً . فثبت بما ذكرنا : أن العلوم إما تصورات وإما تصديقات . وثبت : أن كل واحد منها خارج عن قدرة العبد وعن وسعه. قثبت : أن المعارف والعلوم خارجة عن قدرة البشر ، وأن حصولها ليس إلا بخلق الله سبحانه .
The attestation (taṣdīq) when those concepts (taṣawwurāt) are present, necessarily or essentially, the servant has no power over it, nor any choice in it. Because the servant has absolutely no power over those concepts. And when they are present, they necessitate that attestation in a way that the servant has no power over. So on this basis, it is impossible for that attestation to occur by the servant's acquisition (kasb) and his choice. But if the occurrence of attestation upon the occurrence (1) of those concepts is neither necessary nor essential, then that attestation is not knowledge ('ilm) nor certainty (yaqīn), but rather it is a blind belief (iʿtiqād taqlīdī) that a person adopts without cause. And that is also impossible. And whenever a person attempts to cast doubt in himself about it, that is possible, and before that it is not knowledge nor certainty. So it is established from what we have mentioned: that sciences (ʿulūm) are either concepts (taṣawwurāt) or attestations (taṣdīqāt). And it is established that each one of them is outside the power (qudra) of the servant and beyond his capacity (wusʿ). So it is established that cognitions (maʿārif) and sciences are outside the capacity of man, and that their occurrence is only by the creation (khalq) of Allah, Exalted.
Source pages p. 108

Commentary
This passage presents an argument demonstrating that knowledge is not within human power. The author considers two cases regarding the occurrence of attestation (taṣdīq) when concepts are present. First, if the attestation is necessary or essential upon the presence of those concepts, then the servant has no power over it, because the concepts themselves are not under his control. Thus, the attestation cannot be by his acquisition or choice. Second, if the attestation is not necessary, then it is not genuine knowledge or certainty but a blind belief adopted without cause, which is also impossible. Moreover, such a belief can be doubted, so it is not certain. The author concludes that all sciences, whether concepts or attestations, are beyond human power and are created only by Allah.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Knowledge is acquired by human effort alone.
Correction: The passage shows that genuine knowledge is either forced by concepts (not chosen) or is merely blind belief (not real knowledge), so it cannot be acquired by human power.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, if the attestation (taṣdīq) is not necessary upon the presence of concepts, what is it considered?
A) Genuine knowledge
B) Certainty
C) Blind belief
D) Impossible

Key Takeaway
All genuine knowledge, whether concepts or attestations, is created by Allah and is beyond human power.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If every genuine belief you hold is created by Allah and not by your own effort, how does that reshape your attitude towards learning and pride in your knowledge? Will you thank Him for your understanding rather than credit yourself?
?
Consider a conviction you have that you cannot rationally justify—could it be blind belief (taqlīd) rather than true certainty? What would it take for you to cast doubt on it and see if it withstands, and would that risk destroying something you thought was knowledge?
?
When you feel doubt about an Islamic teaching, does that reveal that your belief was not true knowledge? How would you respond to that doubt in light of this lesson, knowing that doubt can expose a lack of certainty? Will you seek the kind of necessary knowledge that leaves no room for doubt?
